Pentecostal</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr></tbody></table></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" width="20" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/200px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" width="200" height="387" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/300px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/400px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="2127" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The oldest known icon of <i><a href="/wiki/Christ_Pantocrator_(Sinai)" title="Christ Pantocrator (Sinai)">Christ Pantocrator</a></i> (6th century A.D.), <a href="/wiki/Encaustic_painting" title="Encaustic painting">encaustic on panel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Catherine%27s_Monastery" title="Saint Catherine&#39;s Monastery">Saint Catherine\'s Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Eastern Orthodox Church</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-brit_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GOAA_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOAA-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> officially the <b>Orthodox Catholic Church</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations_by_number_of_members" title="List of Christian denominations by number of members">second-largest Christian church</a>, with over 250 million members.<sup id="cite_ref-adherentsWeb2_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherentsWeb2-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-about.com_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about.com-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> As one of the oldest religious institutions in the world, the Orthodox Church has played a prominent role in the history and culture of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Southeastern Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare19938_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare19938-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It operates as a <a href="/wiki/Communion_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Communion (Christian)">communion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> churches, each governed by its bishops, called a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Synod" title="Holy Synod">Holy Synod</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GOAA_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOAA-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The church has no central doctrinal or governance authority analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">pope</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople</a> is recognised by all as <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i> ("first among equals") of the bishops.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_theology" title="Eastern Orthodox theology">Eastern Orthodox theology</a> is based on the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, and the church teaches that it is the <a href="/wiki/Four_Marks_of_the_Church" title="Four Marks of the Church">One, Holy, Catholic and Apostolic</a> <a href="/wiki/One_true_church" title="One true church">church</a> established by <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus Christ</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a>, and that its bishops are the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">successors</a> of Christ\'s <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It maintains that it practices the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Christian_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox Christian theology">original Christian faith</a>, passed down by <a href="/wiki/Sacred_tradition" title="Sacred tradition">sacred tradition</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Patriarchates" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchates">patriarchates</a>, reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Pentarchy" title="Pentarchy">pentarchy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Autocephalous" class="mw-redirect" title="Autocephalous">autocephalous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Autonomous" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous">autonomous</a> churches reflect a variety of <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical" class="mw-redirect" title="Hierarchical">hierarchical</a> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church_organization" title="Eastern Orthodox Church organization">organisation</a>. Of its innumerable <a href="/wiki/Sacred_mysteries#Eastern_Christianity" title="Sacred mysteries">Sacred Mysteries</a>, it recognises seven "major sacraments," of which the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> is the principal one, celebrated <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">liturgically</a> in <a href="/wiki/Synaxis" title="Synaxis">synaxis</a>. The church teaches that through <a href="/wiki/Consecration#Eucharist" title="Consecration">consecration</a> <a href="/wiki/Epiclesis" title="Epiclesis">invoked</a> by a <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Orthodox_Church)" title="Priesthood (Orthodox Church)">priest</a> the sacrificial bread and wine become the body and blood of Christ. The <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Virgin Mary</a> is <a href="/wiki/Veneration" title="Veneration">venerated</a> in the Eastern Orthodox Church as the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Mother of God</a>, honoured in <a href="/wiki/Marian_devotions" title="Marian devotions">devotions</a>.\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox Church shared <a href="/wiki/Communion_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Communion (Christian)">communion</a> with the Catholic Church until the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism">East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism</a> in AD 1054, triggered by disputes over doctrine, especially the <a href="/wiki/Papal_primacy" title="Papal primacy">authority of the Pope</a>. Before the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> in AD 451, <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Oriental Orthodox churches">Oriental Orthodox churches</a> also shared in this communion, separating primarily over <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Definition" title="Chalcedonian Definition">differences in Christology</a>.\n</p><p>The majority of Eastern Orthodox Christians live in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, with smaller communities in the former Byzantine regions of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">eastern Mediterranean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and to a decreasing degree also in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians" title="Persecution of Christians">persecution</a>. There are also many in other parts of the world, formed through <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">conversions</a>, and missionary activity.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" class="toclevel-3 tocsection-61"><a href="#Books"><span class="tocnumber">12.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Books</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-62"><a class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_characteristics">Name and characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name and characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-citation_style" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may contain <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_overkill" title="Wikipedia:Citation overkill">an excessive number of citations</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please consider <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_overkill#How_to_trim_excessive_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citation overkill">removing references to unnecessary or disreputable sources</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_overkill#Citation_Merging" title="Wikipedia:Citation overkill">merging citations</a> where possible, or, if necessary, <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Bombardment" title="Wikipedia:Bombardment">flagging the content for deletion</a>.</span>  <small><i>(July 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In keeping with the church\'s teaching on universality and with the Nicene Creed, Orthodox authorities such as Saint <a href="/wiki/Raphael_of_Brooklyn" title="Raphael of Brooklyn">Raphael of Brooklyn</a> have insisted that the full name of the church has always included the term "Catholic", as in "Holy Orthodox Catholic Apostolic Church".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECleenewerck2009&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoeKOUb6OcG4CpgPA101Holy_101&#93;_&quot;The_Church_of_the_East_has_never_from_the_first_been_known_by_any_other_name_than_Catholic,_nor_has_she_set_aside_this_title_in_any_official_document.&quot;_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECleenewerck2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoeKOUb6OcG4CpgPA101Holy_101]_&quot;The_Church_of_the_East_has_never_from_the_first_been_known_by_any_other_name_than_Catholic,_nor_has_she_set_aside_this_title_in_any_official_document.&quot;-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The official name of the Eastern Orthodox Church is the "Orthodox Catholic Church".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Brittanica_Online&#91;httpwwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;&#93;_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy,_official_name,_used_in_British_English_as_well,_is_Orthodox_Catholic_Church,_one_of_the_three_major_doctrinal_and_jurisdictional_groups_of_Christianity.&quot;_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Brittanica_Online[httpwwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;]_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy,_official_name,_used_in_British_English_as_well,_is_Orthodox_Catholic_Church,_one_of_the_three_major_doctrinal_and_jurisdictional_groups_of_Christianity.&quot;-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllwood_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid1pGbdI4L0qsCpgPA128_128&#93;_&quot;The_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches_are_properly_known_as_the_&quot;Orthodox_Catholic_Church&quot;&quot;_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllwood_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid1pGbdI4L0qsCpgPA128_128]_&quot;The_Eastern_Orthodox_Churches_are_properly_known_as_the_&quot;Orthodox_Catholic_Church&quot;&quot;-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Losch2002_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Losch2002-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> It is the name by which the church refers to itself in its liturgical or canonical texts,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Brittanica_Online&#91;httpwwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;&#93;_&quot;The_official_designations_of_the_church_in_its_liturgical_or_canonical_texts_are_either_&quot;the_Orthodox_Catholic_Church&quot;_or_the_&quot;Greek_Catholic_Church&quot;_only.&quot;_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Brittanica_Online[httpwwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;]_&quot;The_official_designations_of_the_church_in_its_liturgical_or_canonical_texts_are_either_&quot;the_Orthodox_Catholic_Church&quot;_or_the_&quot;Greek_Catholic_Church&quot;_only.&quot;-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309ffalsevonepageqffalse_309&#93;_&quot;The_official_designation_of_the_church_in_Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_or_canonical_texts_is_&quot;the_Orthodox_Catholic_Church.&quot;&quot;_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309ffalsevonepageqffalse_309]_&quot;The_official_designation_of_the_church_in_Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_or_canonical_texts_is_&quot;the_Orthodox_Catholic_Church.&quot;&quot;-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> in official publications,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrthodox_Eastern_Church1909_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrthodox_Eastern_Church1909-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreek_Orthodox_Church1875_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreek_Orthodox_Church1875-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and in official contexts or administrative documents.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Orthodox teachers refer to the Church as Catholic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993307_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993307-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzgerald1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecomid_F5yntZocGICpgPA8_8&#93;_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzgerald1998[httpsbooksgooglecomid_F5yntZocGICpgPA8_8]-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This name and longer variants containing "Catholic" are also recognised and referenced in other books and publications by secular or non-Orthodox writers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Vie1945_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Vie1945-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENielsenJohnsonEllis2001248_&quot;In_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Catholic_Church,_authority_...&quot;_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENielsenJohnsonEllis2001248_&quot;In_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Catholic_Church,_authority_...&quot;-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortescue1908&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidUPr1ZCxPW6QCpgPA255_255&#93;_&quot;..._it_is_all_gathered_together_and_still_lives_in_the_Holy_Apostolic_Orthodox_Catholic_Church_of_the_Seven_Councils.&quot;_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortescue1908[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidUPr1ZCxPW6QCpgPA255_255]_&quot;..._it_is_all_gathered_together_and_still_lives_in_the_Holy_Apostolic_Orthodox_Catholic_Church_of_the_Seven_Councils.&quot;-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchad\xc3\xa9_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions2006_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchad\xc3\xa9_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions2006-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELosch2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecomidOdKCVjCschMCpgPA76_76&#93;_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELosch2002[httpsbooksgooglecomidOdKCVjCschMCpgPA76_76]-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309_309-10&#93;_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Encyclopedia_of_World_Religions1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidZP_f9icf2roCpg309_309-10]-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The common name of the Church, "Eastern Orthodox Church", is a shortened practicality that helps to avoid confusions in casual use. From ancient times through the first millennium, Greek was the most prevalent shared language in the demographic regions where the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> flourished, and Greek, being the language in which the New Testament was written, was the primary liturgical language of the church. For this reason, the eastern churches were sometimes identified as "Greek" (in contrast to the "Roman" or "Latin" church, which used a Latin translation of the Bible), even before the great schism. After 1054, "Greek Orthodox" or "Greek Catholic" marked a church as being in communion with Constantinople, much as "Catholic" did for communion with Rome. This identification with Greek, however, became increasingly confusing with time. Missionaries brought Orthodoxy to many regions without ethnic Greeks, where the Greek language was not spoken. In addition, struggles between Rome and Constantinople to control parts of southeastern Europe resulted in the conversion of some churches to Rome, which then also used "Greek Catholic" to indicate their continued use of the Byzantine rites. Today, many of those same churches remain, while a very large number of Orthodox are not of Greek national origin, and do not use Greek as the language of worship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Brittanica_Online&#91;httpwwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;&#93;_&quot;Because_of_the_use_of_the_name_&quot;Greek_Catholics&quot;_by_the_Eastern_churches_of_the_Catholic_Church_and_the_historical_links_of_the_Orthodox_Catholic_church_with_the_Eastern_Roman_Empire_and_Byzantium_(Constantinople),_however,_the_&#91;&#91;exonym&#93;&#93;s_in_American_English_usage_referred_to_it_as_the_&quot;Eastern&quot;_or_&quot;Greek_Orthodox&quot;_Church._These_terms_are_sometimes_misleading,_especially_when_applied_to_Russian_or_Slavic_churches_and_to_the_Orthodox_communities_in_western_Europe_and_America.&quot;_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclop\xc3\xa6dia_Brittanica_Online[httpwwwbritannicacomEBcheckedtopic177174Eastern-Orthodoxy_&quot;Eastern_Orthodoxy&quot;]_&quot;Because_of_the_use_of_the_name_&quot;Greek_Catholics&quot;_by_the_Eastern_churches_of_the_Catholic_Church_and_the_historical_links_of_the_Orthodox_Catholic_church_with_the_Eastern_Roman_Empire_and_Byzantium_(Constantinople),_however,_the_[[exonym]]s_in_American_English_usage_referred_to_it_as_the_&quot;Eastern&quot;_or_&quot;Greek_Orthodox&quot;_Church._These_terms_are_sometimes_misleading,_especially_when_applied_to_Russian_or_Slavic_churches_and_to_the_Orthodox_communities_in_western_Europe_and_America.&quot;-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> "Eastern", then, indicates the geographical element in the Church\'s origin and development, while "Orthodox" indicates the faith, as well as communion with the Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople.<sup id="cite_ref-Calivas1983_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calivas1983-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> There are additional Christian churches in the east that are in communion with neither Rome nor Constantinople, who tend to be distinguished by the category named "Oriental Orthodox". While the Church continues officially to call itself "Catholic", for reasons of universality, the common title of "Eastern Orthodox Church" avoids casual confusion with the Catholic Church.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Orthodoxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg/220px-Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg/330px-Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg/440px-Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1576" data-file-height="2027" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantinischer_Mosaizist_um_1000_002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Constantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Constantine">Emperor Constantine</a> presents a representation of the city of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> as tribute to an enthroned Mary and baby Jesus in this church mosaic. <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, c. 1000).</div></div></div>\n<p>The first known use of the phrase "the catholic church" (<i>he katholike ekklesia</i>) occurred in a letter written about 110 AD from one Greek church to another (<a href="/wiki/Saint_Ignatius_of_Antioch" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Ignatius of Antioch">Saint Ignatius of Antioch</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrnaeans</a>) The letter states: "Wheresoever the bishop shall appear, there let the people be, even as where Jesus may be, there is the universal [katholike] Church."<sup id="cite_ref-CathEnc1910_Catholic_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CathEnc1910_Catholic-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Thus, almost from the very beginning, Christians referred to the Church as the "One, Holy, <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Catholic" class="extiw" title="wikt:Catholic">Catholic</a> (from the Greek \xce\xba\xce\xb1\xce\xb8\xce\xbf\xce\xbb\xce\xb9\xce\xba\xce\xae, or "according to the whole, universal") and Apostolic Church".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardon1981&#91;httpsbooksgooglecomid3Ke37zpSv3gCpgPA217_217&#93;_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardon1981[httpsbooksgooglecomid3Ke37zpSv3gCpgPA217_217]-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The Eastern Orthodox Church claims that it is today the continuation and preservation of that same Church.\n</p><p>A number of other Christian churches also make a similar claim: the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodox</a>. In the Eastern Orthodox view, the Assyrians and Orientals left the Orthodox Church in the years following the Third Ecumenical <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a> (431) and the Fourth Ecumenical <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> (451), respectively, in their refusal to accept those councils\' <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christological</a> definitions. Similarly, the churches in Rome and Constantinople separated in an event known as the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism">East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism</a>, traditionally dated to the year 1054, although it was more a gradual process than a sudden break. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> separated from the Catholic Church, not directly from the Eastern Orthodox Church, for the first time in the 1530s (and, after a brief reunion in 1555, again finally in 1558). Thus, though it was united to Orthodoxy when established through the work of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Augustine_of_Canterbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Augustine of Canterbury">Saint Augustine of Canterbury</a> in the early 7th century, its separation from Orthodoxy came about indirectly through the See of Rome.\n</p><p>To all these churches, the claim to catholicity (universality, oneness with the ancient church) is important for multiple doctrinal reasons that have more bearing internally in each church than in their relation to the others, now separated in faith. The meaning of holding to a faith that is true is the primary reason why anyone\'s statement of which church split off from which other has any significance at all; the issues go as deep as the schisms. The depth of this meaning in the Eastern Orthodox Church is registered first in its use of the word "<a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodox</a>" itself, a union of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>orthos</i> ("straight", "correct", "true", "right") and <i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a></i> ("common belief", from the ancient verb \xce\xb4\xce\xbf\xce\xba\xce\xad\xcf\x89-\xce\xb4\xce\xbf\xce\xba\xe1\xbf\xb6 which is translated "to believe", "to think", "to consider", "to imagine", "to assume").<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The dual meanings of <i>doxa</i>, with "glory" or "glorification" (of God by the Church and of the Church by God), especially in worship, yield the pair "correct belief" and "true worship". Together, these express the core of a fundamental teaching about the inseparability of belief and worship and their role in drawing the Church together with Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare199116,_271_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare199116,_271-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199869-72_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199869-72-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgarian</a> and all the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a> churches use the title <i>Pravoslavie</i> (<a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Cyrillic alphabets">Cyrillic</a>: \xd0\x9f\xd1\x80\xd0\xb0\xd0\xb2\xd0\xbe\xd1\x81\xd0\xbb\xd0\xb0\xd0\xb2\xd0\xb8\xd0\xb5), meaning "correctness of glorification", to denote what is in English <i>Orthodoxy</i>, while the Georgians use the title <i>Martlmadidebeli</i>. Several other churches in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> also came to use <i>Orthodox</i> in their titles, but are still distinct from the Eastern Orthodox Church as described in this article.\n</p><p>The term "Eastern Church" (the geographic east in the East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism) has been used to distinguish it from western <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> (the geographic West, which at first came to designate the Catholic communion, later also the various Protestant and Anglican branches). "Eastern" is used to indicate that the highest concentrations of the Eastern Orthodox Church presence remain in the eastern part of the Christian world, although it is growing worldwide. Orthodox Christians throughout the world use various ethnic or national jurisdictional titles, or more inclusively, the title "Eastern Orthodox", "Orthodox Catholic", or simply "Orthodox".<sup id="cite_ref-Calivas1983_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calivas1983-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>What unites Orthodox Christians is the catholic faith as carried through Holy Tradition. That faith is expressed most fundamentally in Scripture and in worship,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991212-213_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991212-213-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and the latter most essentially through the Mystery of Baptism and in the Divine Liturgy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991282_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991282-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The faith lives and breathes by God\'s energies in communion with the Church. Inter-communion is the litmus test by which all can see that two churches share the same faith; lack of inter-communion (excommunication, literally "out of communion") is the sign of different faiths, even though some central theological points may be shared. The sharing of beliefs can be highly significant, but it is not the full measure of the faith.\n</p><p>The lines of even this test can blur, however, when differences that arise are not due to doctrine, but to recognition of jurisdiction. As the Eastern Orthodox Church has spread into the west and over the world, the church as a whole has yet to sort out all the inter-jurisdictional issues that have arisen in the expansion, leaving some areas of doubt about what is proper church governance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991180-199_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991180-199-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> And as in the ancient church persecutions, the aftermath of modern persecutions of Christians in communist nations has left behind both some governance and some faith issues that have yet to be completely resolved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991152-179_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991152-179-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>All members of the Eastern Orthodox Church profess the same faith, regardless of race or nationality, jurisdiction or local custom, or century of birth. Holy Tradition encompasses the understandings and means by which that unity of faith is transmitted across boundaries of time, geography, and culture. It is a continuity that exists only inasmuch as it lives within Christians themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991203-204_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991203-204-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> It is not static, nor an observation of rules, but rather a sharing of observations that spring both from within and also in keeping with others, even others who lived lives long past. The Holy Spirit maintains the unity and consistency of the Holy Tradition to preserve the integrity of the faith within the Church, as given in the Scriptural promises.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The shared beliefs of Orthodoxy, and its theology, exist within Holy Tradition and cannot be separated from it, for their meaning is not expressed in mere words alone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991215_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991215-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Doctrine cannot be understood unless it is prayed.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Doctrine must also be lived in order to be prayed, for without action, the prayer is idle and empty, a mere vanity, and therefore the theology of demons.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> According to these teachings of the ancient church, no superficial belief can ever be orthodox. Similarly, reconciliation and unity are not superficial, but are prayed and lived out.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholicity">Catholicity<span id="Catholicity_of_the_Orthodox_Church"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Catholicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg/200px-John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg" width="200" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg/300px-John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg/400px-John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2554" data-file-height="3825" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_the_Baptist_of_Macedonia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> of Saint <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>, 14th century, Republic of Macedonia</div></div></div>\n<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church considers itself to be both <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_(term)" title="Catholic (term)">catholic</a>. The doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Catholicity_of_the_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicity of the Church">Catholicity of the Church</a>, as derived from the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, is essential to Eastern Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiology" title="Ecclesiology">ecclesiology</a>. The term <i>Catholicity of the Church</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\x9a\xce\xb1\xce\xb8\xce\xbf\xce\xbb\xce\xb9\xce\xba\xcf\x8c\xcf\x84\xce\xb7\xcf\x82 \xcf\x84\xe1\xbf\x86\xcf\x82 \xe1\xbc\x98\xce\xba\xce\xba\xce\xbb\xce\xb7\xcf\x83\xce\xaf\xce\xb1\xcf\x82</span>) is used in its original sense, as a designation for the <a href="/wiki/Universality_of_the_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Universality of the Church">Universality of the Church</a>, centered around Christ. Therefore, the Eastern Orthodox notion of <a href="/wiki/Catholicity" title="Catholicity">catholicity</a> is not centered around any singular see, unlike Catholicism, that has one earthly center.\n</p><p>Due to the influence of the Catholic Church in the west, where the English language itself developed, the words "catholic" and "catholicity" are sometimes used to refer to that church specifically. However, the more prominent dictionary sense given for general use is still the one shared by other languages, implying breadth and universality, reflecting comprehensive scope.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmerican_Heritage_Dict5th_ed294_&#91;httpahdictionarycomwordsearchhtmlqcatholicity_&quot;catholicity&quot;&#93;_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmerican_Heritage_Dict5th_ed294_[httpahdictionarycomwordsearchhtmlqcatholicity_&quot;catholicity&quot;]-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In a Christian context, the Christian Church, as identified with the original church founded by Christ and his apostles, is said to be catholic (or universal) in regard to its union with Christ in faith. Just as Christ is indivisible, so are union with Him and faith in him, whereby the church is "universal", unseparated, and comprehensive, including all who share that faith. Orthodox bishop <a href="/wiki/Kallistos_Ware" title="Kallistos Ware">Kallistos Ware</a> has called that "simple Christianity".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare199116_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare199116-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> That is the sense of early and <a href="/wiki/Patristic" class="mw-redirect" title="Patristic">patristic</a> usage wherein the church usually refers to itself as the "Catholic Church",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_of_Christianity2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecomid7ly4DgtT3LkCpgPA867_867&#93;_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEncyclopedia_of_Christianity2003[httpsbooksgooglecomid7ly4DgtT3LkCpgPA867_867]-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeith1982&#91;httpsbooksgooglecomidsaFgJRjaPwcCpgPA486_486&#93;_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeith1982[httpsbooksgooglecomidsaFgJRjaPwcCpgPA486_486]-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> whose faith is the "Orthodox faith". It is also the sense within the phrase "<a href="/wiki/One,_Holy,_Catholic,_and_Apostolic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="One, Holy, Catholic, and Apostolic Church">One, Holy, Catholic, and Apostolic Church</a>", found in the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, and referred to in Orthodox worship, e.g. in the litany of the catechumens in the <a href="/wiki/Divine_liturgy" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine liturgy">divine liturgy</a>.\n</p><p>With the mutual excommunications of the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism">East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism</a> in 1054, the churches in Rome and Constantinople each viewed the other as having departed from the <a href="/wiki/One_true_church" title="One true church">true church</a>, leaving a smaller but still-catholic church in place. Each retained the "Catholic" part of its title, the "<a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>" (or the "Roman Catholic Church") on the one hand, and the "Orthodox Catholic Church" on the other, each of which was defined in terms of inter-communion with either Rome or Constantinople. While the Eastern Orthodox Church recognizes what it shares in common with other churches, including the Catholic Church, it sees catholicity in terms of complete union in communion and faith, with the church throughout all time, and the sharing remains incomplete when not shared fully.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_leadership">Organization and leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Organization and leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Church organization">Orthodox Church organization</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG/180px-Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG" width="180" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG/270px-Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG/360px-Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stgeorgeistanbul3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The exterior of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._George,_Istanbul" class="mw-redirect" title="Church of St. George, Istanbul">Patriarchal Basilica of St. George</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Patriarchate of Constantinople</a>, located in the Fener (<i>Fanari</i>) district of Istanbul.</div></div></div>\n<p>The religious authority for Eastern Orthodoxy is not a Patriarch or the Pope as in Catholicism, nor the Bible as in <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, but the scriptures as interpreted by the seven ecumenical councils of the Church. The Eastern Orthodox Church is a fellowship of "<a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> for self-headed) Churches, with the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople</a> being the only autocephalous head who holds the title <i><a href="/wiki/First_among_equals" class="mw-redirect" title="First among equals">primus inter pares</a></i>, meaning "first among equals" in <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>. The Patriarch of Constantinople has the honor of primacy, but his title is only first among equals and has no real authority over Churches other than the Constantinopolitan.<sup id="cite_ref-brit_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-religionfacts_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-religionfacts-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The Eastern Orthodox Church considers <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> Christ to be the head of the Church and the Church to be his body. It is believed that authority and the <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">grace of God</a> is directly passed down to Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Laying_on_of_hands" title="Laying on of hands">laying on of hands</a>\xe2\x80\x94a practice started by the <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostles</a>, and that this unbroken historical and physical link is an essential element of the true Church (Acts 8:17, 1 Tim 4:14, Heb 6:2). However, the Church asserts that apostolic succession also requires apostolic faith, and bishops without apostolic faith, who are in <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>, forfeit their claim to apostolic succession.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox communion is organized into several regional churches, which are either autocephalous ("self-headed") or lower ranking <a href="/wiki/Autonomous" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous">autonomous</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> term for "self-lawed") church bodies unified in <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and worship. These include the fourteen autocephalous churches of <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Patriarchate_of_Antioch_and_All_the_East" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Patriarchate of Antioch and All the East">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Patriarchate_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Patriarchate of Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgian_Orthodox_Church" title="Georgian Orthodox Church">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Cyprus" title="Church of Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Orthodox_Church" title="Bulgarian Orthodox Church">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Orthodox_Church" title="Serbian Orthodox Church">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Greece" title="Church of Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_Orthodox_Church" title="Polish Orthodox Church">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Romanian Orthodox Church">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Albanian Orthodox Church">Albania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Czech_and_Slovak_Orthodox_Church" title="Czech and Slovak Orthodox Church">Czech Republic and Slovakia</a>, which were officially invited to the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Orthodox_Council" title="Pan-Orthodox Council">Pan-Orthodox Council</a> of 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-council2016_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-council2016-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> as well as a number of autonomous Churches.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Each church has a ruling <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Synod" title="Holy Synod">Holy Synod</a> to administer its jurisdiction and to lead the Orthodox Church in the preservation and teaching of the apostolic and <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/patristic" class="extiw" title="wikt:patristic">patristic</a> traditions and church practices.\n</p><p>Each bishop has a territory (<a href="/wiki/Episcopal_see" title="Episcopal see">see</a>) over which he governs.<sup id="cite_ref-religionfacts_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-religionfacts-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> His main duty is to make sure the traditions and practices of the Orthodox Church are preserved. Bishops are equal in authority and cannot interfere in the jurisdiction of another bishop. Administratively, these bishops and their territories are organized into various <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> groups or <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synods</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> who gather together at least twice a year to discuss the state of affairs within their respective sees. While bishops and their autocephalous synods have the ability to administer guidance in individual cases, their actions do not usually set precedents that affect the entire Eastern Orthodox Church. Bishops are almost always chosen from the monastic ranks and must remain unmarried.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_councils">Church councils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Church councils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There have been a number of times when alternative theological ideas arose to challenge the Orthodox faith. At such times the Orthodox communion deemed it necessary to convene a general or <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">"great" council</a> of all available bishops throughout the world. The Orthodox Church holds that <a href="/wiki/First_seven_Ecumenical_Councils" class="mw-redirect" title="First seven Ecumenical Councils">seven ecumenical councils</a>, held between the 4th and the 8th centuries, are authoritative.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg/200px-Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg" width="200" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg/300px-Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg/400px-Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1318" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rylands_Nicene_Creed_papyrus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oldest extant manuscript of the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, dated to the 5th Century</div></div></div>\n<p>The ecumenical councils followed a democratic form, with each bishop having one vote. Though present and allowed to speak before the council, members of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Imperial Roman</a>/<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> court, abbots, priests, deacons, monks and laymen were not allowed to vote. The primary goal of these Great Synods was to verify and confirm the fundamental beliefs of the Great Christian Church as truth, and to remove as heresy any false teachings that would threaten the Church. The <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope of Rome</a> at that time held the position of "<a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">first among equals</a>" and, while he was not present at any of the councils, he continued to hold this title until the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism">East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism</a> of 1054.\n</p><p>Other councils have helped to define the Eastern Orthodox position, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Quinisext_Council" title="Quinisext Council">Quinisext Council</a>, the Synods of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Eastern_Orthodox)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox)">879\xe2\x80\x93880</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Fifth Council of Constantinople">1341, 1347, 1351</a>, 1583, 1819, and <a href="/wiki/Phyletism" title="Phyletism">1872</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jassy" title="Synod of Jassy">Synod of Jassy</a> (Ia\xc8\x99i), 1642, and the Pan-Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem_(1672)" title="Synod of Jerusalem (1672)">Synod of Jerusalem</a>, 1672; the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Orthodox_Council" title="Pan-Orthodox Council">Pan-Orthodox Council</a>, held in Greece in 2016, was the only such Eastern Orthodox council in modern times.\n</p><p>According to Orthodox teaching the position of "First Among Equals" gives no additional power or authority to the bishop that holds it, but rather that this person sits as organizational head of a council of equals (like a president). His words and opinions carry no more insight or wisdom than any other bishop. It is believed that the Holy Spirit guides the Eastern Orthodox Church through the decisions of the entire council, not one individual. Additionally it is understood that even the council\xe2\x80\x99s decisions must be accepted by the entire Orthodox Church in order for them to be valid.\n</p><p>One of the decisions made by the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a> (the second ecumenical council, meeting in 381) and supported by later such councils was that the Patriarch of Constantinople should be given equal honor to the Pope of Rome since Constantinople was considered to be the "<a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">New Rome</a>". According to the third Canon of the second ecumenical council: "Because it is new Rome, the bishop of Constantinople is to enjoy the privileges of honor <i>after</i> the bishop of Rome." This means that both enjoy the same privileges because they are both bishops of the imperial capitals, but the bishop of Rome will precede the bishop of Constantinople since Old Rome precedes New Rome.\n</p><p>The 28th canon of the fourth ecumenical council clarified this point by stating: "For the Fathers rightly granted privileges to the throne of Old Rome because it was the royal city. And the One Hundred and Fifty most religious Bishops (i.e. the second ecumenical council in 381) actuated by the same consideration, gave equal privileges to the most holy throne of New Rome, justly judging that the city which is honored with the Sovereignty and the Senate, and enjoys equal privileges with the old imperial Rome, should in ecclesiastical matters also be magnified as she is."\n</p><p>Because of the schism the Eastern Orthodox no longer recognize the primacy of the Pope of Rome. The Patriarch of Constantinople therefore, like the Pope before him, now enjoys the title of "first among equals".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherents">Adherents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Adherents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country" title="Eastern Orthodoxy by country">Eastern Orthodoxy by country</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png/450px-Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png" width="450" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png/675px-Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png/900px-Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="882" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Distribution of Eastern Orthodox Christians in the world by country:</b> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #000055; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;More than 75%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000aa; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;50\xe2\x80\x9375%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;20\xe2\x80\x9350%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #5555ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;5\xe2\x80\x9320%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #aaaaff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1\xe2\x80\x935%</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b4c8ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Below 1%, but has local <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephaly</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>Politics, wars, persecutions, oppressions, and related potential threats<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ14Apr15_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ14Apr15-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> can make precise counts of Orthodox membership difficult to obtain at best in some regions. Historically, forced migrations have also altered demographics in relatively short periods of time. The most reliable estimates currently available number Orthodox adherents at around 200 million worldwide,<sup id="cite_ref-brit_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> making Eastern Orthodoxy the second largest Christian communion in the world after the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-adherentsWeb_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherentsWeb-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The numerous <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> groups in the world, if taken all together, outnumber the Eastern Orthodox,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiamondPlattnerCostopoulos2005&#91;httpwwwworldcatorgtitleworld-religions-and-democracyoclc58807255viewport_119&#93;_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiamondPlattnerCostopoulos2005[httpwwwworldcatorgtitleworld-religions-and-democracyoclc58807255viewport_119]-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> but they differ theologically and do not form a single communion.<sup id="cite_ref-adherentsWeb_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherentsWeb-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> According to the 2015 Yearbook of International Religious Demography, the Eastern Orthodox population in 2010 decreased to 4% of the global population from 7.1% of the global population in 1910. According to the same source, in terms of the total Christian population, the relative percentages were 12.2% and 20.4% respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Yearbook_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yearbook-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, the Eastern Orthodox share of the world\'s total Christian population was 12% in 2011.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="This statement is inconsistent with other parts of the article. (September 2015)">inconsistent</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint selfreference">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Talk:Eastern_Orthodox_Church#Pew_global_Christianity_report" title="Talk:Eastern Orthodox Church">discuss</a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(<span>p21</span>)</sup>\n</p><p>Most members today are concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asian_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian Russia">Asian Russia</a>, in addition to significant minorities in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, although Eastern Orthodoxy has spread into a global religion towards Western Europe and the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, with churches in most countries and major cities. The adherents constitute the largest single religious <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> in the world\'s largest country \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sreda2012_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sreda2012-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> where roughly half of Eastern Orthodox Christians live. They are the majority religion in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Razumkov2006_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Razumkov2006-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Belarus2011_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belarus2011-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Macedonia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> they also dominate in the disputed territories <a href="/wiki/Abkhazia" title="Abkhazia">Abkhazia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Ossetia" title="South Ossetia">South Ossetia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transnistria" title="Transnistria">Transnistria</a>. Significant minorities of Eastern Orthodox are present in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Tieslietu_Ministrija_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tieslietu_Ministrija-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-2013table28_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013table28-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-freedom_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedom-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-freedom2_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-freedom2-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and many other countries.\n</p><p>The percentage of <a href="/wiki/Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Christians">Christians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> fell from 19 percent in 1914 to 2.5 percent in 1927,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> predominately due to persecution, including the <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a> and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">population exchange between Greece and Turkey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> population exchanges between Bulgaria and Turkey, and associated <a href="/wiki/Christian_emigration" title="Christian emigration">emigration of Christians</a> to foreign countries (mostly in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="The Americas">the Americas</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Today there are more than 160,000 people (about 0.2%) of different <a href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIAWeb_66-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWeb-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through mostly labor migration from Eastern Europe and some conversion, Orthodox Christianity is the fastest growing religious grouping in certain Western countries, for example in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-H\xc3\xa4mmerliMayer2014_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H\xc3\xa4mmerliMayer2014-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AugheyOakland2013_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AugheyOakland2013-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> but Orthodoxy is not "a central marker of minority identity" for the migrants.<sup id="cite_ref-H\xc3\xa4mmerliMayer2014_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H\xc3\xa4mmerliMayer2014-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> While in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the number of Orthodox parishes is growing.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2010_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2010-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Christian_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox Christian theology">Eastern Orthodox Christian theology</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinity">Trinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Trinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Orthodox Christians believe in the Holy <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, three distinct, divine persons (<i><a href="/wiki/Hypostasis_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypostasis (philosophy)">hypostases</a></i>), without overlap or <a href="/wiki/Sabellianism" title="Sabellianism">modality</a> among them, who share one divine <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> (<i>ousia</i> Greek \xce\xbf\xe1\xbd\x90\xcf\x83\xce\xaf\xce\xb1)\xe2\x80\x94uncreated, immaterial and <a href="/wiki/Eternity" title="Eternity">eternal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993208-211_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993208-211-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> These three persons are typically distinguished by their relation to each other. The Father is eternal and not begotten and does not proceed from any, the Son is eternal and begotten of the Father, and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a> is eternal and proceeds from the Father. Orthodox doctrine regarding the Holy Trinity is summarised in the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed#Niceno\xe2\x80\x93Constantinopolitan_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993202_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993202-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In discussing God\'s relationship to His creation, Orthodox theology <a href="/wiki/Essence%E2%80%93Energies_distinction" class="mw-redirect" title="Essence\xe2\x80\x93Energies distinction">distinguishes</a> between God\'s eternal essence, which is totally transcendent, and His <i>uncreated energies</i>, which is how He reaches us. The God who is transcendent and the God who touches us are one and the same. That is, these energies are not something that proceed from God or that God produces, but rather they are God himself: distinct, yet inseparable from, God\'s inner being.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare199367\xe2\x80\x9369_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare199367\xe2\x80\x9369-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In understanding the Holy Trinity as "one God in three persons", "three persons" is not to be emphasized more than "one God", and vice versa. While the three persons are distinct, they are united in one divine essence, and their oneness is expressed in community and action so completely that they cannot be considered separately. For example, their salvation of mankind is an activity engaged in common: "Christ became man by the good will of the Father and by the cooperation of the Holy Spirit. Christ sends the Holy Spirit who proceeds from the Father, and the Holy Spirit forms Christ in our hearts, and thus God the Father is glorified." Their "communion of essence" is "indivisible". Trinitarian terminology\xe2\x80\x94essence, hypostasis, etc.\xe2\x80\x94are used "philosophically", "to answer the ideas of the heretics", and "to place the terms where they separate error and truth."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998128\xe2\x80\x93130_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998128\xe2\x80\x93130-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The words do what they can do, but the nature of the Trinity in its fullness remains beyond man\'s comprehension and expression, a Holy Mystery that can only be experienced.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Sin.2C_salvation.2C_and_the_incarnation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sin,_salvation,_and_the_incarnation">Sin, salvation, and the incarnation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sin, salvation, and the incarnation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SanGiovanniDamasceno.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/SanGiovanniDamasceno.jpg/200px-SanGiovanniDamasceno.jpg" width="200" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/SanGiovanniDamasceno.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="222" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SanGiovanniDamasceno.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_of_Damascus" title="John of Damascus">John of Damascus</a></div></div></div>\n<p>According to the Eastern Orthodox faith, at some point in the beginnings of human existence, humanity was faced with a choice: to learn the difference between good and evil through observation or through participation. The biblical story of <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">Adam and Eve</a> relates this choice by mankind to participate in evil, accomplished through disobedience to God\'s command. Both the intent and the action were separate from God\'s will; it is that separation that defines and marks any operation as sin. The separation from God caused the loss of (fall from) his grace, a severing of mankind from his creator and the source of his life. The end result was the diminishment of human nature and its subjection to death and corruption, an event commonly referred to as the "fall of man".\n</p><p>When Orthodox Christians refer to Fallen Nature they are not saying that human nature has become evil in itself. Human nature is still formed in the image of God; we are still God\'s creation, and God has never created anything evil. But our fallen nature remains open to evil intents and actions. It is sometimes said that we are "inclined to sin"; that is, we find some sinful things attractive. It is the nature of temptation to make sinful things seem the more attractive, and it is the fallen nature of humans that seeks or succumbs to the attraction. Orthodox Christians reject the Augustinian position that the descendants of Adam and Eve are actually guilty of the original sin of their ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> But just as any species begets its own kind, so fallen humans beget fallen humans, and from the beginning of our existence we lie open to sinning by our own choice.\n</p><p>Since the fall of man, then, it has been mankind\'s dilemma that no human can restore his nature to union with God\'s grace; it was necessary for God to effect another change in human nature. Orthodox Christians believe that Christ Jesus was both God and Man absolutely and completely, having two natures indivisibly: eternally begotten of the Father in his divinity, he was born in his humanity of a woman, Mary, by her consent, through descent of the Holy Spirit. He lived on earth, in time and history, as a man. As a man he also died, and went to the place of the dead, which is Hades. But being God, neither death nor Hades could contain him, and he rose to life again, in his humanity, by the power of the Holy Spirit, thus destroying the power of Hades and of death itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrysostom400&#91;&#91;Paschal_Homily&#93;&#93;_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrysostom400[[Paschal_Homily]]-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Through God\'s participation in humanity, Christ\'s human nature, perfected and unified with his divine nature, ascended into heaven, there to reign in communion with the Father and Holy Spirit.\n</p><p>By these acts of salvation, Christ provided fallen mankind with the path to escape its fallen nature. The Eastern Orthodox Church teaches that through baptism into Christ\'s death, and our death unto sin in repentance, with God\'s help we can also rise with Christ into heaven, healed of the breach of our fallen nature and restored to God\'s grace. To Orthodox Christians, this process is what is meant by "salvation," which consists of the Christian life. The ultimate goal is <a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Orthodox_theology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theosis (Eastern Orthodox theology)">theosis</a> \xe2\x80\x93 an even closer union with God and closer likeness to God than existed in the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a>. This process is called <a href="/wiki/Divinization_(Christian)" title="Divinization (Christian)">Deification</a> or "God became man that man might become \'god\'". However, it must be emphasized that Orthodox Christians do not believe that man becomes God in His essence, or a god in his own nature. More accurately, Christ\'s salvific work enables man in his human nature to become "partakers of the Divine nature" (2 Peter 1:4); that is to say, man is united to God in Christ.\n</p><p>Through Christ\'s destruction of Hades\' power to hold humanity hostage, he made the path to salvation effective for all the righteous who had died from the beginning of time \xe2\x80\x93 saving many, including Adam and Eve, who are remembered in the Church as saints.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESt._Athanasius318&#91;httpwwwcopticchurchnettopicstheologyincarnation_st_athanasiuspdf_Ch._2-3&#93;_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt._Athanasius318[httpwwwcopticchurchnettopicstheologyincarnation_st_athanasiuspdf_Ch._2-3]-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox reject the idea that Christ died to give God "satisfaction" as taught by <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm</a>, or as a punitive substitute as taught by the Reformers. Sin (separation from God, who is the source of all life) is its own punishment, capable of imprisoning the soul in an existence without life, without anything good, and without hope: hell by any measure. Life on earth is God\'s gift, to give us opportunity to make our choice real: separation or union.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurrection_of_Christ">Resurrection of Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Resurrection of Christ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg/200px-1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg"
