width="200" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg/300px-1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg/400px-1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1678._%D0%A1%D0%B0%D1%88%D1%8D%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B5_%D1%9E_%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BB%D0%B0.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox</a> icon of the Resurrection.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church understands the death and resurrection of Christ Jesus to be real historical events, as described in the gospels of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>. Jesus Christ, the Son of God, was in his humanity (that is, in history) crucified, and died, descending into Hades (<a href="/wiki/Sheol" title="Sheol">Sheol</a>), the place of the dead, as all humans do. But He, alone among humans, has two natures, one human, one divine, which are indivisible and inseparable from each other through the Mystery of the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">incarnation</a>. Hades could not restrain the infinite God. Christ in His divine nature <a href="/wiki/Harrowing_of_Hell" title="Harrowing of Hell">captured the keys of Hades</a> and broke the bonds which had imprisoned the human souls who had been held there through their separation from God.\n</p><p>Neither could death contain the Son of God, the Fountain of Life, who arose from death even in his human nature. Not only this, but he opened the gates of Hades to all the righteous dead of past ages, rescuing them from their fallen human nature and restoring them to a nature of grace with God, bringing them back to life, this time in God\'s heavenly kingdom. And this path he opened to all who choose to follow him in time yet to come, thus saving the human race. Thus the Eastern Orthodox proclaim each year at the time of Pascha (Easter), that Christ "trampled down death by death, and on those in the tombs bestowed life."\n</p><p>The celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">Resurrection</a> of Christ at Pascha is the central event in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_calendar" title="Eastern Orthodox liturgical calendar">liturgical year</a> of the Eastern Orthodox Church. According to Orthodox tradition, each human being may partake of this immortality, which would have been impossible without the Resurrection; it is the main promise held out by God in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>. Every holy day of the Eastern Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Liturgical" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical">liturgical</a> year relates to the Resurrection directly or indirectly. Every Sunday is especially dedicated to celebrating the Resurrection and the triune God, representing a mini-Pascha. In the liturgical commemorations of the <a href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Passion (Christianity)">Passion of Christ</a> during <a href="/wiki/Holy_Week" title="Holy Week">Holy Week</a> there are frequent allusions to the ultimate victory at its completion.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_life">Christian life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Christian life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Church teaching is that Orthodox Christians, through baptism, enter a new life of salvation through repentance whose purpose is to share in the life of God through the work of the Holy Spirit. The Eastern Orthodox Christian life is a spiritual pilgrimage in which each person, through the <a href="/wiki/Imitation_of_Christ" title="Imitation of Christ">imitation of Christ</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">hesychasm</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998234-237,(241=Glossary)_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos1998234-237,(241=Glossary)-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> cultivates the practice of unceasing prayer. Each life occurs within the life of the Church as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_Christ" title="Body of Christ">body of Christ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006&#91;httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_34&#93;_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006[httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_34]-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> It is then through the fire of God\'s love in the action of the Holy Spirit that each member becomes more holy, more wholly unified with Christ, starting in this life and continuing in the next.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOxford_Dict_Christian_Church3rd_ed_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOxford_Dict_Christian_Church3rd_ed-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The Church teaches that everyone, being born in God\'s image, is called to theosis, fulfillment of the image in likeness to God. God the creator, having divinity by nature, offers each person participation in divinity by cooperatively accepting His gift of grace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006&#91;httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_21&#93;_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeorge2006[httporthodoxinfocomgeneraltheosis-englishpdf_21]-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox Church, in understanding itself to be the Body of Christ, and similarly in understanding the Christian life to lead to the unification in Christ of all members of his body, views the church as embracing all Christ\'s members, those now living on earth, and also all those through the ages who have passed on to the heavenly life. The church includes the Christian saints from all times, and also judges, prophets and righteous Jews of the first covenant, Adam and Eve, even the angels and heavenly hosts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199825-30_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199825-30-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In Orthodox services, the earthly members together with the heavenly members worship God as one community in Christ, in a union that transcends time and space and joins heaven to earth. This unity of the Church is sometimes called the <i>communion of the saints</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199823_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHierotheos199823-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mother_of_God_and_saints">Mother of God and saints</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mother of God and saints">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg/220px-Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg/330px-Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg/440px-Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tinos_panagia_evangelistria_200707_04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Tinos" title="Our Lady of Tinos">Our Lady of Tinos</a> is the major <a href="/wiki/Marian_shrine" class="mw-redirect" title="Marian shrine">Marian shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vladimirskaya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vladimirskaya.jpg/170px-Vladimirskaya.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vladimirskaya.jpg/255px-Vladimirskaya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Vladimirskaya.jpg/340px-Vladimirskaya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="456" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vladimirskaya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Theotokos_of_Vladimir" title="Theotokos of Vladimir">Theotokos of Vladimir</a>, one of the most venerated of Orthodox Christian icons of the <a href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church believes death and the separation of body and soul to be unnatural\xe2\x80\x94a result of the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Man" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of Man">Fall of Man</a>. They also hold that the congregation of the Church comprises both the living and the dead. All persons currently in heaven are considered to be <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, whether their names are known or not. There are, however, those saints of distinction whom God has revealed as particularly good examples. When a saint is revealed and ultimately recognised by a large portion of the Church a service of official recognition (<a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">canonization</a>) is celebrated.\n</p><p>This does not \'make\' the person a saint; it merely recognizes the fact and announces it to the rest of the Church. A day is prescribed for the saint\xe2\x80\x99s celebration, hymns composed and icons created. Numerous saints are celebrated on each day of the year. They are venerated (shown great respect and love) but not worshiped, for worship is due God alone. In showing the saints this love and requesting their prayers, the Eastern Orthodox manifest their belief that the saints thus assist in the process of salvation for others.\n</p><p>Pre-eminent among the saints is the <a href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mother_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother of God">Mother of God</a>. In Orthodox theology, the Mother of God is the fulfillment of the Old Testament archetypes revealed in the <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">Ark of the Covenant</a> (because she carried the New Covenant in the person of Christ) and the <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">burning bush</a> that appeared before <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> (symbolizing the Mother of God\'s carrying of God without being consumed<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>). Accordingly, the Eastern Orthodox consider Mary to be the Ark of the New Covenant and give her the respect and reverence as such. The Theotokos was chosen by God and she freely co-operated in that choice to be the Mother of Jesus Christ, the God-man.\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox believe that the Christ Child from the moment of conception was both fully God and fully human. Mary is thus called the \'Theotokos\' or \'Bogoroditsa\' as an affirmation of the divinity of the one to whom she gave birth. It is also believed that her virginity was not compromised in conceiving God-incarnate, that she was not harmed and that she remained forever a virgin. Scriptural references to "brothers" of Christ are interpreted as kin, given that the word \'brother\' was used in multiple ways, as was the term \'father\'. Due to her unique place in salvation history, Mary is honored above all other saints and especially venerated for the great work that God accomplished through her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993257-258_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993257-258-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox Church regards the bodies of all saints as holy, made such by participation in the Holy Mysteries, especially the communion of Christ\'s holy body and blood, and by the indwelling of the Holy Spirit within the Church. Indeed, that persons and physical things can be made holy is a cornerstone of the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">Incarnation</a>, made manifest also directly by God in Old Testament times through his dwelling in the Ark of the Covenant. Thus, physical items connected with saints are also regarded as holy, through their participation in the earthly works of those saints. God himself bears witness to this holiness of saints\' <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> through the many miracles connected with them that have been reported throughout history since Biblical times, often including healing from disease and injury.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993234_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993234-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eschatology">Eschatology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Eschatology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg/200px-Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg/300px-Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg/400px-Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2760" data-file-height="3684" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Torcello_-_Santa_Maria_Assunta.Last_Judgement.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Last Judgment</i>: 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> mosaic from <a href="/wiki/Torcello" title="Torcello">Torcello</a> Cathedral.</div></div></div>\n<p>Orthodox Christians believe that when a person dies the soul is temporarily separated from the body. Though it may linger for a short period on Earth, it is ultimately escorted either to paradise (<a href="/wiki/Abraham%27s_bosom" class="mw-redirect" title="Abraham&#39;s bosom">Abraham\'s bosom</a>) or the darkness of <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hades" title="Christian views on Hades">Hades</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Particular_judgment" title="Particular judgment">Temporary Judgment</a>. Orthodox do not accept the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">Purgatory</a>, which is held by Catholicism. The soul\xe2\x80\x99s experience of either of these states is only a "foretaste"\xe2\x80\x94being experienced only by the soul\xe2\x80\x94until the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Final Judgment</a>, when the soul and body will be reunited.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-soulafterdeath_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soulafterdeath-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox believe that the state of the soul in Hades can be affected by the love and prayers of the righteous up until the Last Judgment.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> For this reason the Church offers a special <a href="/wiki/Prayer_for_the_dead" title="Prayer for the dead">prayer for the dead</a> on the third day, ninth day, fortieth day, and the one-year anniversary after the death of an Orthodox Christian. There are also several days throughout the year that are set aside for general commemoration of the departed, sometimes including nonbelievers. These days usually fall on a Saturday, since it was on a Saturday that Christ lay in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Tomb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-soulafterdeath_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soulafterdeath-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg/200px-Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg/300px-Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg/400px-Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1277" data-file-height="850" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ph.Patmos_Monastery-02Wiki.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Monastery_of_Saint_John_the_Theologian" title="Monastery of Saint John the Theologian">Monastery of Saint John the Theologian</a> in <a href="/wiki/Patmos" title="Patmos">Patmos</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> was written</div></div></div>\n<p>While the Eastern Orthodox consider the text of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Apocalypse</a> (Book of Revelation) to be a part of Scripture, it is also regarded to be a Mystery. Speculation on the contents of Revelation are minimal and it is never read as part of the regular order of services.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Those theologians who have delved into its pages tend to be <a href="/wiki/Amillennialism" title="Amillennialism">amillennialist</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatology</a>, believing that the "thousand years" spoken of in biblical prophecy refers to the present time: from the <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">Crucifixion of Christ</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming_of_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Coming of Christ">Second Coming</a>.\n</p><p>While it is not usually taught in church it is often used as a reminder of God\xe2\x80\x99s promise to those who love Him, and of the benefits of avoiding sinful passions. Iconographic depictions of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Final Judgment</a> are often portrayed on the back (western) wall of the church building to remind the departing faithful to be vigilant in their struggle against sin. Likewise it is often painted on the walls of the Trapeza (refectory) in a monastery where monks may be inspired to sobriety and detachment from worldly things while they eat.\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox believe that <a href="/wiki/Hell_in_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Hell in Christianity">Hell</a>, though often described in metaphor as punishment inflicted by God, is in reality the soul\'s rejection of God\'s infinite love which is offered freely and abundantly to everyone.\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox believe that after the Final Judgment:\n</p>\n<ul><li>All souls will be reunited with their <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_Dead" class="mw-redirect" title="Resurrection of the Dead">resurrected bodies</a>.</li>\n<li>All souls will fully experience their spiritual state.</li>\n<li>Having been perfected, the saints will forever progress towards a deeper and fuller love of God, which equates with eternal happiness.<sup id="cite_ref-soulafterdeath_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soulafterdeath-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bible">Bible</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bible">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The official bible of the Eastern Orthodox Church contains the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> text of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Book of Daniel</a> given in the translation by <a href="/wiki/Theodotion" title="Theodotion">Theodotion</a>. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Patriarchal_Text&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Patriarchal Text (page does not exist)">Patriarchal Text</a> is used for the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Orthodox Christians hold that the Bible is a verbal icon of Christ, as proclaimed by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">7th ecumenical council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991209_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991209-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> They refer to the Bible as Holy Scripture, meaning writings containing the foundational truths of the Christian faith as revealed by Christ and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> to its divinely inspired human authors. Holy Scripture forms the primary and authoritative written witness of <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Tradition">Holy Tradition</a> and is essential as the basis for all Orthodox teaching and belief.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991209,_(quoting_&#91;&#91;John_Chrysostom&#124;St._John_Chrysostom&#93;&#93;):_&quot;It_is_impossible_for_a_man_to_be_saved_if_he_does_not_read_the_Scriptures.&quot;_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991209,_(quoting_[[John_Chrysostom|St._John_Chrysostom]]):_&quot;It_is_impossible_for_a_man_to_be_saved_if_he_does_not_read_the_Scriptures.&quot;-111">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The Bible provides the only texts held to be suitable for reading in Orthodox worship services. Through the many scriptural quotations embedded in the worship service texts themselves, it is often said that the Eastern Orthodox pray the Bible as well as read it.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg/200px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg" width="200" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg/300px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg/400px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg 2x" data-file-width="465" data-file-height="514" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol5v.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>David glorified by the women of Israel</i> from the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Psalter" title="Paris Psalter">Paris Psalter</a>, example of the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_art_(Byzantine)" title="Macedonian art (Byzantine)">Macedonian art (Byzantine)</a> (sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Renaissance" title="Macedonian Renaissance">Macedonian Renaissance</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/St._Jerome" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Jerome">St. Jerome</a> completed the well-known <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> Latin translation only in the early 5th century, around the time the accepted lists of scripture were resolved in the west. The east took up to a century longer to resolve the lists in use there, and ended by accepting a few additional writings from the Septuagint that did not appear in the lists of the west. The differences were small and were not considered to compromise the unity of the faith shared between east and west. They did not play a role in the eventual <a href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" class="mw-redirect" title="East-West Schism">schism in the 11th century</a> that separated the See of Rome and the West from the See of Constantinople and the other apostolic Orthodox Churches, and remained as defined essentially without controversy in the East or West for at least one thousand years. It was only in the 16th century that <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation Protestants</a> challenged the lists, proclaiming a <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">canon</a> that rejected those Old Testament books that did not appear in the 3rd-century <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>. In response, the Catholic and Eastern Orthodox churches reaffirmed their accepted scriptural lists in more formal canons of their own.\n</p><p>Once established as Holy Scripture, there has never been any question that the Eastern Orthodox Church holds the full list of books to be venerable and beneficial for reading and study,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> even though it informally holds some books in higher esteem than others, the four gospels highest of all. Of the subgroups significant enough to be named, the "<a href="/wiki/Anagignoskomena" class="mw-redirect" title="Anagignoskomena">Anagignoskomena</a>" (\xe1\xbc\x80\xce\xbd\xce\xb1\xce\xb3\xce\xb9\xce\xb3\xce\xbd\xcf\x89\xcf\x83\xce\xba\xcf\x8c\xce\xbc\xce\xb5\xce\xbd\xce\xb1, "things that are read") comprises ten of the Old Testament books rejected in the Protestant canon,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> but deemed by the Eastern Orthodox worthy to be read in worship services, even though they carry a lesser esteem than the 39 books of the Hebrew canon.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The lowest tier contains the remaining books not accepted by either Protestants or Catholics, among them, <a href="/wiki/Psalm_151" title="Psalm 151">Psalm 151</a>. Though it is a psalm, and is in the book of psalms, it is not classified as being within the Psalter (the first 150 psalms),<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and hence does not participate in the various liturgical and prayer uses of the Psalter.\n</p><p>In a very strict sense, it is not entirely orthodox to call the Holy Scriptures the "Word of God". That is a title the Eastern Orthodox Church reserves for Christ, as supported in the scriptures themselves, most explicitly in the first chapter of the gospel of John. God\'s Word is not hollow, like human words. "God said, \'let there be light\'; and there was light."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> This is the Word which spoke the universe into being, and resonates in creation without diminution throughout all history, a Word of divine power.\n</p><p>As much as the Eastern Orthodox Church reveres and depends on the scriptures, they cannot compare to the Word of God\'s manifest action. But the Eastern Orthodox do believe that the Holy Scriptures testify to God\'s manifest actions in history, and that through its divine inspiration God\'s Word is manifested both in the scriptures themselves and in the cooperative human participation that composed them. It is in that sense that the Eastern Orthodox refer to the scriptures as "God\'s Word".\n</p><p>The Eastern Orthodox Church does not subscribe to the Protestant doctrine of <i><a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a></i>. The Church has defined what Scripture is; it also interprets what its meaning is.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Christ promised: "When He, the Spirit of truth, has come, He will guide you into all truth".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Holy Spirit, then, is the infallible guide for the Church to the interpretation of Scripture. The Church depends upon those saints who, by lives lived in <a href="/wiki/Imitation_of_Christ" title="Imitation of Christ">imitation of Christ</a>, achieving <a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Orthodox_theology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theosis (Eastern Orthodox theology)">theosis</a>, can serve as reliable witnesses to the Holy Spirit\'s guidance. Individual interpretation occurs within the Church and is informed by the Church. It is rational and reasoned, but is not arrived at only by means of deductive reasoning.\n</p><p>Scriptures are understood to contain historical fact, poetry, idiom, metaphor, simile, moral fable, parable, prophecy and <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_literature" title="Wisdom literature">wisdom literature</a>, and each bears its own consideration in its interpretation. While divinely inspired, the text stills consists of words in human languages, arranged in humanly recognizable forms. The Eastern Orthodox Church does not oppose honest critical and historical study of the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-Ware1991pp210-5_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ware1991pp210-5-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In biblical interpretation, it does not use speculations, suggestive theories, or incomplete indications, not going beyond what is fully known.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_tradition_and_the_patristic_consensus">Holy tradition and the patristic consensus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Holy tradition and the patristic consensus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>"That faith which has been believed everywhere, always, and by all", the faith taught by Jesus to the apostles, given life by the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> at <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>, and passed down to future generations without additions and without subtractions, is known as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Tradition">Holy Tradition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993195-196_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993195-196-120">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Holy Tradition does not change in the Eastern Orthodox Church because it encompasses those things that do not change: the nature of the one God in Trinity, Father, Son, and Holy Spirit, the history of God\'s interactions with his peoples, the Law as given to the Israelites, all Christ\'s teaching as given to the disciples and Jews and recorded in scripture, including the parables, the prophecies, the miracles, and His own example to humanity in His extreme humility. It encompasses also the worship of the church, which grew out of the worship of the synagogue and temple and was extended by Christ at the last supper, and the relationship between God and His people which that worship expresses, which is also evidenced between Christ and his disciples. It includes the authority that Christ bestowed on his disciples when he made them apostles,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> for the preserving and teaching of the faith, and for governing the organization and conduct of the church (in its administration by bishops).\n</p><p>Holy Tradition is firm, even unyielding, but not rigid or legalistic; instead, it lives and breathes within the Church.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> For example, the New Testament was entirely written by the early church (mostly the apostles). The whole Bible was accepted as scripture by means of Holy Tradition practiced within the early church. The writing and acceptance took five centuries, by which time the Holy Scriptures themselves had become in their entirety a part of Holy Tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991205_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991205-124">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> But Holy Tradition did not change, because "that faith which has been believed everywhere, always, and by all" remained consistent, without additions, and without subtractions. The historical development of the Divine Liturgy and other worship services and devotional practices of the church provide a similar example of extension and growth "without change".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1991213_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1991213-125">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The continuity and stability of Orthodox worship throughout the centuries is one means by which Holy Tradition expresses the unity of the whole church throughout time. Not only can the Eastern Orthodox of today visit a church in a place that speaks a language unknown to the visitors yet have the service remain familiar and understandable to them, but the same would hold true were any able to visit past eras. The church strives to preserve Holy Tradition "unchanging" that it may express the one unchanging faith for all time to come as well.\n</p><p>Besides these, Holy Tradition includes the doctrinal definitions and statements of faith of the seven ecumenical councils, including the <a href="/wiki/Nicene-Constantinopolitan_Creed" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicene-Constantinopolitan Creed">Nicene-Constantinopolitan Creed</a>, and some later local councils, patristic writings, canon law, and icons.<sup id="cite_ref-Ware1991pp210-5_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ware1991pp210-5-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Not all portions of Holy Tradition are held to be equally strong. Some, the Holy Scriptures foremost, certain aspects of worship, especially in the Divine Liturgy, the doctrines of the ecumenical councils, the <a href="/wiki/Nicene-Constantinopolitan_Creed" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicene-Constantinopolitan Creed">Nicene-Constantinopolitan Creed</a>, possess a verified authority that endures forever, irrevocably.<sup id="cite_ref-Ware1991pp210-5_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ware1991pp210-5-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> But with local councils and patristic writings, the Church applies a selective judgement. Some councils and writers have occasionally fallen into error, and some contradict each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Ware1991pp210-5_119-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ware1991pp210-5-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In other cases, opinions differ, no consensus is forthcoming, and all are free to choose. With agreement among the Fathers, though, the authority of interpretation grows, and full patristic consensus is very strong. With canon law (which tends to be highly rigorous and very strict, especially with clergy) an unalterable validity also does not apply, since canons deal with living on earth, where conditions are always changing and each case is subject to almost infinite variation from the next.<sup id="cite_ref-Ware1991pp210-5_119-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ware1991pp210-5-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Even when and where they were once used with full strictness, their application was not absolute, and was carried out for individuals under the pastoral care of their bishop, who had the authority to decide when individual discipline had been satisfied. This too is a part of the Holy Tradition.\n</p><p>By tradition, the Eastern Orthodox Church, when faced with issues that are larger than a single bishop can resolve, holds a local council. The bishops and such others as may attend convene (as St. Paul called the Corinthians to do) to seek the <i>mind of the church</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> A council\'s declarations or edicts then reflect its consensus (if one can be found). An ecumencial council is only called for issues of such import or difficulty or pervasiveness that smaller councils are insufficient to address them. Ecumenical councils\' declarations and canons carry binding weight by virtue of their representation across the whole church, by which the mind of the church can be readily seen. However, not all issues are so difficult as to require an ecumenical council to resolve. Some doctrines or decisions, not defined in a formal statement or proclaimed officially, nevertheless are held by the Church unshakably and unanimously without internal disturbance, and these, also reflecting the mind of the church, are just as firmly irrevocable as a formal declaration of an ecumenical council. Lack of formality does not imply lack of authority within Holy Tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Ware1991pp210-5_119-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ware1991pp210-5-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> An example of such unanimity can be found in the acceptance in the 5th century of the lists of books that comprise Holy Scripture, a true canon without official stamp.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_expansion_and_doctrinal_integrity">Territorial expansion and doctrinal integrity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Territorial expansion and doctrinal integrity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the course of the early church, there were numerous followers who attached themselves to the Christ and His mission here on Earth, as well as followers who retained the distinct duty of being commissioned with preserving the quality of life and lessons revealed through the experience of Jesus living, dying, resurrecting and ascending among them. As a matter of practical distinction and logistics, people of varying gifts were accorded stations within the community structure \xe2\x80\x93 ranging from the host of agape meals (shared with brotherly and fatherly love), to prophecy and the reading of Scripture, to preaching and interpretations and giving aid to the sick and the poor. Sometime after Pentecost the Church grew to a point where it was no longer possible for the Apostles to minister alone. Overseers (bishops)<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and assistants (deacons and deaconesses)<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> were appointed to further the mission of the Church.\n</p><p>The ecclesia recognised the gathering of these early church communities as being greatest in areas of the known world that were famous for their significance on the world stage \xe2\x80\x93 either as hotbeds of intellectual discourse, high volumes of trade, or proximity to the original sacred sites. These locations were targeted by the early apostles, who recognised the need for humanitarian efforts in these large urban centers and sought to bring as many people as possible into the ecclesia \xe2\x80\x93 such a life was seen as a form of deliverance from the decadent lifestyles promoted throughout the eastern and western Roman empire.\n</p><p>As the Church increased in size through the centuries, the logistic dynamics of operating such large entities shifted: patriarchs, metropolitans, archimandrites, abbots and abbesses, all rose up to cover certain points of administration.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a result of heightened exposure and popularity of the philosophical schools (haereseis) of Greco-Roman society and education, Synods and Councils were forced to engage such schools that sought to co-opt the language and pretext of the Christian faith in order to gain power and popularity for their own political and cultural expansion. As a result, ecumenical councils were held to attempt to rebuild solidarity by using the strength of distant orthodox witnesses to dampen the intense local effects of particular philosophical schools within a given area.\n</p><p>While originally intended to serve as an internal check and balance for the defense of the doctrine developed and spread by the apostles to the various sees against faulty local doctrine, at times the church found its own bishops and emperors falling prey to local conventions. At these crucial moments in the history of the church, it found itself able to rebuild on the basis of the faith as it was kept and maintained by monastic communities, who subsisted without reliance on the community of the state or popular culture and were generally unaffected by the materialism and rhetoric that often dominated and threatened the integrity and stability of the urban churches.\n</p><p>In this sense, the aim of the councils was not to expand or fuel a popular need for a clearer or relevant picture of the original apostolic teaching. Rather, the theologians spoke to address the issues of external schools of thought who wished to distort the simplicity and neutrality of the apostolic teaching for personal or political gain. The consistency of the Eastern Orthodox faith is entirely dependent on the Holy Tradition of the accepted corpus of belief \xe2\x80\x93 the decisions ratified by the fathers of the <a href="/wiki/First_seven_Ecumenical_Councils" class="mw-redirect" title="First seven Ecumenical Councils">seven ecumenical councils</a>, and this is only done at the beginning of a consecutive council so that the effects of the decisions of the prior council can be audited and verified as being both conceptually sound and pragmatically feasible and beneficial for the church as a whole.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship">Worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Rite" title="Byzantine Rite">Byzantine Rite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_worship" title="Eastern Orthodox worship">Eastern Orthodox worship</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Western_Rite_Orthodoxy" title="Western Rite Orthodoxy">Western Rite Orthodoxy</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg/200px-Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg" width="200" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg/300px-Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg/400px-Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1576" data-file-height="2020" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meister_der_Sophien-Kathedrale_von_Ohrid_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">Fresco</a> of <a href="/wiki/Basil_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Basil the Great">Basil the Great</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedral</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohrid" title="Ohrid">Ohrid</a>. The saint is shown <a href="/wiki/Consecration" title="Consecration">consecrating</a> the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Mysteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Mysteries">Gifts</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a> which bears his name.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_calendar">Church calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Church calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The minority of autocephalous Orthodox churches, but the majority of the members of the Eastern Orthodox Church, follow the Julian calendar <sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The majority of Orthodox autocephalous churches follow the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Julian_calendar" title="Revised Julian calendar">Revised Julian calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The autonomous Church of Finland of the Ecumenical Patriarchate, as well as parts of the Church of the Czech Lands and Slovakia, use the Gregorian calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many church traditions, including the schedules of services, feasts, and fasts, are structured by the church\xe2\x80\x99s calendar, which provides a strictly observed intermingled set of cycles of varying lengths. The fixed annual cycle begins on 1 September and establishes the times for all annual observances that are fixed by date, such as Christmas. The annual Paschal cycle is established relative to the varying date of Pascha each year and affects the times for such observances as <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Pascha</a> itself, <a href="/wiki/Great_Lent" title="Great Lent">Great Lent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holy_Week" title="Holy Week">Holy Week</a>, and the feasts of <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Ascension" title="Feast of the Ascension">Ascension</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>.\n</p><p>\nLesser cycles also run in tandem with the annual ones. A weekly cycle of days prescribes a specific focus for each day in addition to others that may be observed.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup></p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Each day of the Weekly Cycle is dedicated to certain special memorials. Sunday is dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">Christ\'s Resurrection</a>; Monday honors the holy bodiless powers (angels, archangels, etc.); Tuesday is dedicated to the prophets and especially the greatest of the prophets, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">St. John the Forerunner and Baptist of the Lord</a>; Wednesday is consecrated to the Cross and recalls Judas\' betrayal; Thursday honors the holy apostles and hierarchs, especially <a href="/wiki/Saint_Nicholas" title="Saint Nicholas">St. Nicholas, Bishop of Myra in Lycia</a>; Friday is also consecrated to the Cross and recalls the day of the Crucifixion; Saturday is dedicated to All Saints, especially the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Mother of God</a>, and to the memory of all those who have departed this life in the hope of resurrection and eternal life.\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_services">Church services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Church services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Canonical_Hours#Orthodox_and_Greek-Catholic_usage" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical Hours">Canonical Hours \xc2\xa7&#160;Orthodox and Greek-Catholic usage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Divine_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine Services">Divine Services</a></div>\n<p>The services of the church are conducted each day according to the church calendar. Parts of each service remain fixed, while others change depending on the observances prescribed for the specific day in the various cycles, ever providing a flow of constancy within variation. Services are conducted in the church and involve both the clergy and faithful. Services cannot properly be conducted by a single person, but must have at least one other person present (i.e. a Priest cannot celebrate alone, but must have at least a Chanter present and participating).\n</p><p>Usually, all of the services are conducted on a daily basis only in monasteries and cathedrals, while parish churches might only do the services on the weekend and major feast days. On certain <a href="/wiki/Great_Feasts" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Feasts">Great Feasts</a> (and, according to some traditions, every Sunday) a special <a href="/wiki/All-Night_Vigil" class="mw-redirect" title="All-Night Vigil">All-Night Vigil</a> (<i>Agrypnia</i>) will be celebrated from late at night on the eve of the feast until early the next morning. Because of its festal nature it is usually followed by a breakfast feast shared together by the congregation.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The journey is to the Kingdom. This is where we are going\xe2\x80\x94not symbolically, but really.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Fr. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Schmemann" title="Alexander Schmemann">Alexander Schmemann</a>, <i>For the Life of the World</i></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We knew not whether we were in heaven or on earth.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Ambassadors of Kievan Rus (10th Century), <i>Apocryphal quote from <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Christianization of Kievan Rus&#39;">conversion of Kievan Rus</a></i>.</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg/200px-Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg" width="200" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg/300px-Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg/400px-Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1576" data-file-height="2158" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meister_der_Palastkapelle_in_Palermo_003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">Icon</a> of Ss. <a href="/wiki/Basil_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Basil the Great">Basil the Great</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a>, ascribed authors of the two most frequently used <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> Divine Liturgies, c. 1150 (<a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cappella_Palatina" title="Cappella Palatina">Palatine Chapel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>Services, especially the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a>, may only be celebrated once a day on a single altar (some churches have multiple altars in order to accommodate large congregations). Each priest may only celebrate the Divine Liturgy once a day.\n</p><p>From its Jewish roots, the liturgical day begins at sundown. The traditional daily cycle of services is as follows:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vespers" title="Vespers">Vespers</a> \xe2\x80\x93 (Greek <i>Hesperinos</i>) Sundown, the beginning of the liturgical day.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Compline" title="Compline">Compline</a> (Greek <i>Apodeipnon</i>, lit. "After-supper") \xe2\x80\x93 After the evening meal, and before sleeping.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Midnight_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Midnight Office">Midnight Office</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Usually served only in monasteries.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Matins" title="Matins">Matins</a> (Greek <i>Orthros</i>) \xe2\x80\x93 First service of the morning. Prescribed to start before sunrise.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Canonical_Hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical Hours">Hours</a> \xe2\x80\x93 First, Third, Sixth, and Ninth \xe2\x80\x93 Sung either at their appropriate times, or in aggregate at other customary times of convenience. If the latter, The First Hour is sung immediately following Orthros, the Third and Sixth before the Divine Liturgy, and the Ninth before Vespers.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a> \xe2\x80\x93 The <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> service. (Called <a href="/wiki/Holy_Mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Mass">Holy Mass</a> in the Western Rite)</li></ul>\n<p>The Divine Liturgy is the celebration of the Eucharist. Although it is usually celebrated between the Sixth and Ninth Hours, it is not considered to be part of the daily cycle of services, as it occurs outside the normal time of the world. The Divine Liturgy is not celebrated on weekdays during <a href="/wiki/Great_Lent" title="Great Lent">Great Lent</a>, and in some places during the lesser fasting seasons either; however, reserve communion is prepared on Sundays and is distributed during the week at the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy_of_the_Presanctified_Gifts" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgy of the Presanctified Gifts">Liturgy of the Presanctified Gifts</a>.\n</p><p>Other items brought to the altar during the Divine Liturgy include a gold or silver chalice with red wine, a small metallic urn of warm water, a metallic communion spoon, a little metallic spear, a sponge, a metal disk with cut pieces of bread upon it, and a star, which is a star-shaped piece of metal over which the priest places a cloth covering when transporting the holy gifts to and from the altar. Also found on the altar table is the <a href="/wiki/Antimension" class="mw-redirect" title="Antimension">antimins</a>. The antimins is a silk cloth, signed by the appropriate diocesan bishop, upon which the sanctification of the holy gifts takes place during each Divine Liturgy. The antimins contain the relics of a saint. When a church is consecrated by a bishop, there is a formal service or prayers and sanctification in the name of the Saint that the church is named after. The bishop will also often present a small relic of a saint to place in or on the altar as part of the consecration of a new church.\n</p><p>The book containing liturgically read portions of the four gospels is permanently "enthroned" on the altar table. The Eastern Orthodox bishops, priests, deacons and readers sing/chant specific verses from this Gospel Book on each different day of the year.\n</p><p>This daily cycle services is conceived of as both the sanctification of time (<i><a href="/wiki/Chronos" title="Chronos">chronos</a></i>, the specific times during which they are celebrated), and entry into eternity (<i><a href="/wiki/Kairos" title="Kairos">kairos</a></i>). They consist to a large degree of litanies asking for God\'s mercy on the living and the dead, readings from the <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalter</a> with introductory prayers, <a href="/wiki/Troparion" title="Troparion">troparia</a>, and other prayers and hymns surrounding them. The Psalms are so arranged that when all the services are celebrated the entire <a href="/wiki/Psalter" title="Psalter">Psalter</a> is read through in their course once a week, and twice a week during <a href="/wiki/Great_Lent" title="Great Lent">Great Lent</a> when the services are celebrated in an extended form.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_chanting">Music and chanting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Music and chanting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg/220px-Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg/330px-Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg/440px-Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Patriarchate_Constantinopolis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chanters singing on the <a href="/wiki/Kliros" title="Kliros">kliros</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._George,_Istanbul" class="mw-redirect" title="Church of St. George, Istanbul">Church of St. George</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchate of Constantinople">Patriarchate of Constantinople</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Orthodox services are sung nearly in their entirety. Services consist in part of a dialogue between the clergy and the people (often represented by the choir or the Psaltis <a href="/wiki/Cantor_(church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cantor (church)">Cantor</a>). In each case the prayers are sung or chanted following a prescribed musical form. Almost nothing is read in a normal speaking voice, with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Homily" title="Homily">homily</a> if one is given.\n</p><p>Because the human voice is seen as the most perfect instrument of praise, musical instruments (<a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organs</a>, guitars, etc.) are not generally used to accompany the choir.\n</p><p>The church has developed eight modes or tones (see <a href="/wiki/Octoechos" title="Octoechos">Octoechos</a>) within which a chant may be set, depending on the time of year, feast day, or other considerations of the <a href="/wiki/Typikon" title="Typikon">Typikon</a>. There are numerous versions and styles that are traditional and acceptable and these vary a great deal between cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993238_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993238-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> It is common, especially in the United States, for a choir to learn many different styles and to mix them, singing one response in Greek, then English, then Russian, etc.\n</p><p>In the Russian tradition there have been some famous composers of "unaccompanied" church music, such as <a href="/wiki/Tchaikovsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Tchaikovsky">Tchaikovsky</a> (<a href="/wiki/Liturgy_of_St._John_Chrysostom_(Tchaikovsky)" title="Liturgy of St. John Chrysostom (Tchaikovsky)">Liturgy of St John Chrysostom</a>, op. 41, 1878, and <a href="/wiki/All-Night_Vigil_(Tchaikovsky)" title="All-Night Vigil (Tchaikovsky)">All-Night Vigil</a>, op. 52, 1882) and <a href="/wiki/Rachmaninoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Rachmaninoff">Rachmaninoff</a> (<a href="/wiki/Liturgy_of_St._John_Chrysostom_(Rachmaninoff)" title="Liturgy of St. John Chrysostom (Rachmaninoff)">Liturgy of St John Chrysostom</a>, op. 31, 1910, and <a href="/wiki/All-Night_Vigil_(Rachmaninoff)" title="All-Night Vigil (Rachmaninoff)">All-Night Vigil</a>, op. 37, 1915); and many church tones can likewise be heard influencing their music.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Incense">Incense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Incense">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg/200px-%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg" width="200" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg/300px-%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg/400px-%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="801" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%95%D0%B2%D1%85%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B8%D1%87%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BD.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priest</a></div></div></div>\n<p>As part of the legacy handed down from its Judaic roots, <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a> is used during all services in the Eastern Orthodox Church as an offering of worship to God as it was done in the Jewish First and Second Temples in Jerusalem (Exodus chapter 30). Incense is also prophesied in the book of <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Malachi#1:11" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Malachi">Malachi 1:11</a> as a "pure offering" in the glorification of God by the Gentiles in "every place" where the name of God is regarded as "great". Traditionally, the base of the incense used is the resin of <a href="/wiki/Boswellia_sacra" title="Boswellia sacra">Boswellia sacra</a>, also known as frankincense, but the resin of fir trees has been used as well. It is usually mixed with various floral essential oils giving it a sweet smell.\n</p><p>Incense represents the sweetness of the prayers of the saints rising up to God (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#141:2" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">Psalm 141:2</a>, <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Revelation#5:8" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Revelation">Revelation 5:8</a>, <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Revelation#8:4" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Revelation">8:4</a>). The incense is burned in an ornate golden censer that hangs at the end of three chains representing the Trinity. Two chains represent the human and Godly nature of the Son, one chain for the Father and one chain for the Holy Spirit. The lower cup represents the earth and the upper cup the heaven. In the Greek, Slavic, and Syrian traditions there are 12 bells hung along these chains representing the 12 apostles. There are also 72 links representing 72 evangelists.\n</p><p>The charcoal represents the sinners. Fire signifies the Holy Spirit and frankincense the good deeds. The incense also represents the grace of the Holy Trinity. The censer is used (swung back and forth) by the priest/deacon to venerate all four sides of the altar, the holy gifts, the clergy, the icons, the congregation, and the church structure itself. Incense is also used in the home where the individual will go around the house and "cross" all of the icons saying in Greek: \xe1\xbc\x8d\xce\xb3\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xcf\x82 \xe1\xbd\x81 \xce\x98\xce\xb5\xcf\x8c\xcf\x82, \xe1\xbc\x8d\xce\xb3\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xcf\x82 \xe1\xbc\xb0\xcf\x83\xcf\x87\xcf\x85\xcf\x81\xcf\x8c\xcf\x82, \xe1\xbc\x8d\xce\xb3\xce\xb9\xce\xbf\xcf\x82 \xe1\xbc\x80\xce\xb8\xce\xac\xce\xbd\xce\xb1\xcf\x84\xce\xbf\xcf\x82, \xe1\xbc\x90\xce\xbb\xce\xad\xce\xb7\xcf\x83\xce\xbf\xce\xbd \xe1\xbc\xa1\xce\xbc\xe1\xbe\xb6\xcf\x82, or in English: Holy God, Holy Mighty, Holy Immortal, have mercy on us.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fasting">Fasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Fasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fasting#Orthodoxy_&amp;_Eastern_Catholicism" title="Fasting">Fasting: Orthodoxy &amp; Eastern Catholicism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg/220px-Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg/330px-Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg/440px-Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1870" data-file-height="1518" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cross_being_thrown_at_Theophany.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blessing of the waters and throwing cross; <a href="/wiki/Theophany" title="Theophany">Theophany</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The number of fast days varies from year to year, but in general the Eastern Orthodox Christian can expect to spend a little over half the year <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a> at some level of strictness. There are spiritual, symbolic, and even practical reasons for fasting. In the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_man" title="Fall of man">Fall from Paradise</a> mankind became possessed by a carnal nature; that is to say, became inclined towards the <a href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Passion (Christianity)">passions</a>. Through fasting, Orthodox Christians attempt to return to the relationship of love and obedience to God enjoyed by Adam and Eve in <a href="/wiki/Paradise" title="Paradise">Paradise</a> in their own lives, by refraining from carnal practices, by bridling the tongue (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/James#3:5" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/James">James 3:5\xe2\x80\x936</a>), confession of sins, prayer and almsgiving.\n</p><p>Fasting is seen as purification and the regaining of innocence. It is a practice of learning to temper the body\'s primary desire for food. By learning to temper this basic desire of the body, the practitioner can more readily temper other worldly desires, and thus, become better enabled to draw closer to <a href="/wiki/Christian_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian God">God</a> in the hope of becoming more <a href="/wiki/Deification#In_Eastern_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Deification">Christ-like</a>. Through obedience to the Church and its ascetic practices the Eastern Orthodox Christian seeks to rid himself or herself of the <i>passions</i> (The desires of our fallen carnal nature). All Orthodox Christians are provided with a prescribed set of guidelines. They do not view fasting as a hardship, but rather as a privilege and joy. The teaching of the Church provides both the time and the amount of fasting that is expected as a minimum for every member who chooses to participate. For greater ascesis, some may choose to go without food entirely for a short period of time. A complete three-day fast at the beginning and end of a fasting period is not unusual, and some fast for even longer periods, though this is usually practiced only in monasteries.\n</p><p>In general, fasting means abstaining from meat and meat products, dairy (eggs and cheese) and dairy products, fish, olive oil, and wine. Wine and oil\xe2\x80\x94and, less frequently, fish\xe2\x80\x94are allowed on certain feast days when they happen to fall on a day of fasting; but animal products and dairy are forbidden on fast days, with the exception of "Cheese Fare" week which precedes Great Lent, during which dairy products are allowed. Wine and oil are usually also allowed on Saturdays and Sundays during periods of fast. In some Orthodox traditions, caviar is permitted on <a href="/wiki/Lazarus_Saturday" title="Lazarus Saturday">Lazarus Saturday</a>, the Saturday before Palm Sunday, although the day is otherwise a fast day. Married couples also abstain from sexual activity on fast days so that they may devote themselves fulsomely to prayer (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Corinthians#7:5" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Corinthians">1 Corinthians 7:5</a>).\n</p><p>While it may seem that fasting in the manner set forth by the Church is a strict rule, there are circumstances where a person\'s spiritual guide may allow an <a href="/wiki/Economy_(Eastern_Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Economy (Eastern Christianity)">Economy</a> because of some physical necessity (e.g. those who are pregnant or infirm, the very young and the elderly, or those who have no control over their diet, such as prisoners or soldiers).\n</p><p>The time and type of fast is generally uniform for all Orthodox Christians; the times of fasting are part of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_calendar" title="Eastern Orthodox liturgical calendar">ecclesiastical calendar</a>, and the method of fasting is set by the <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">Holy Canons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Tradition">Sacred Tradition</a>. There are four major fasting periods during the year:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Nativity_Fast" title="Nativity Fast">Nativity Fast</a> (Advent or "Winter Lent") which is the 40 days preceding the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">Nativity of Christ</a> (Christmas), beginning on 15 November and running through 24 December.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Great_Lent" title="Great Lent">Great Lent</a> which consists of the 6 weeks (40 Days) preceding <a href="/wiki/Palm_Sunday" title="Palm Sunday">Palm Sunday</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Holy_Week" title="Holy Week">Great Week</a> which precedes <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Fast" title="Apostles&#39; Fast">Apostles\' Fast</a> which varies in length from 8 days to 6 weeks. It begins on the Monday following <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Day" title="All Saints&#39; Day">All Saints Sunday</a> and extends to the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Saints_Peter_and_Paul" title="Feast of Saints Peter and Paul">Feast of Saints Peter and Paul</a> on 29 June.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Dormition_Fast" class="mw-redirect" title="Dormition Fast">Dormition Fast</a>, a two-week-long Fast preceding the <i><a href="/wiki/Dormition_of_the_Theotokos" class="mw-redirect" title="Dormition of the Theotokos">Dormition of the Theotokos</a></i>, lasting from 1 August through 15 August.</li></ul>\n<p>In addition to these fasting seasons, Orthodox Christians fast on every Wednesday (in commemoration of Christ\'s betrayal by <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas Iscariot</a>), and Friday (in commemoration of Christ\'s <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">Crucifixion</a>) throughout the year. Monastics often fast on Mondays (in imitation of the <a href="/wiki/Angels" class="mw-redirect" title="Angels">Angels</a>, who are commemorated on that day in the weekly cycle, since monastics are striving to lead an angelic life on earth, and angels neither eat nor drink).\n</p><p>Orthodox Christians who are preparing to receive the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> do not eat or drink at all from vespers (sunset) until after taking <a href="/wiki/Holy_Communion" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Communion">Holy Communion</a>. A similar total fast is expected to be kept on the <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Eve" title="Christmas Eve">Eve of Nativity</a>, the Eve of <a href="/wiki/Epiphany_(holiday)" title="Epiphany (holiday)">Theophany</a> (Epiphany), <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday" title="Good Friday">Great Friday</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holy_Saturday" title="Holy Saturday">Holy Saturday</a> for those who can do so. There are other individual days observed as fasts (though not as days of total fasting) no matter what day of the week they fall on, such as the <a href="/wiki/Beheading_of_St._John_the_Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Beheading of St. John the Baptist">Beheading of St. John the Baptist</a> on 29 August and the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Cross#September_14" title="Feast of the Cross">Exaltation of the Holy Cross</a> on 14 September.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Almsgiving">Almsgiving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Almsgiving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>"<a href="/wiki/Almsgiving" class="mw-redirect" title="Almsgiving">Almsgiving</a>", more comprehensively described as "acts of mercy", refers to any giving of oneself in charity to someone who has a need, such as material resources, work, assistance, counsel, support, or kindness. Along with <i>prayer</i> and <i>fasting</i>, it is considered a pillar of the personal spiritual practices of the Eastern Orthodox Christian tradition. Almsgiving is particularly important during periods of fasting, when the Eastern Orthodox believer is expected to share with those in need the monetary savings from his or her decreased consumption. As with fasting, mentioning to others one\'s own virtuous deeds tends to reflect a sinful pride, and may also be considered extremely rude.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditions">Traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monasticism">Monasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Monasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_Orthodox_monasticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Degrees of Orthodox monasticism">Degrees of Orthodox monasticism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg/220px-Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg/330px-Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg/440px-Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Katharinenkloster_Sinai_BW_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Saint_Catherine%27s_Monastery" title="Saint Catherine&#39;s Monastery">Saint Catherine\'s Monastery</a> (6th century), <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church places heavy emphasis and awards a high level of prestige to traditions of monasticism and asceticism with roots in <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> and Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. The most important centres of Christian Orthodox monasticism are <a href="/wiki/Saint_Catherine%27s_Monastery" title="Saint Catherine&#39;s Monastery">Saint Catherine\'s Monastery</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Greece" title="Northern Greece">Northern Greece</a>.\n</p><p>All Orthodox Christians are expected to participate in at least some ascetic works, in response to the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Christ" title="Law of Christ">commandment of Christ</a> to "come, take up the cross, and follow me." (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Mark</a> 10:21 and elsewhere) They are therefore all called to imitate, in one way or another, Christ himself who denied himself to the extent of literally taking up the cross on the way to his voluntary self-sacrifice.
