However, laypeople are not expected to live in extreme <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a> since this is close to impossible while undertaking the normal responsibilities of worldly life.\n</p><p>Those who wish to do this therefore separate themselves from the world and live as <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monastics</a>: monks and nuns. As ascetics <i>par excellence</i>, using the allegorical weapons of prayer and fasting in spiritual warfare against their passions, monastics hold a very special and important place in the Church. This kind of life is often seen as incompatible with any kind of worldly activity including that which is normally regarded as virtuous. Social work, school teaching, and other such work is therefore usually left to laypeople. Ascetics of the Eastern Orthodox Church are recognised by their long hair, and in case of male monks, long beards.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Megaloschema.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Megaloschema.svg/120px-Megaloschema.svg.png" width="120" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Megaloschema.svg/180px-Megaloschema.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Megaloschema.svg/240px-Megaloschema.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="387" data-file-height="674" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Megaloschema.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schema worn by monks</div></div></div>\n<p>There are three main types of monastics. Those who live in monasteries under a common rule are <i>coenobitic</i>. Each monastery may formulate its own rule, and although there are no <a href="/wiki/Religious_order" title="Religious order">religious orders</a> in Orthodoxy some respected monastic centers such as <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a> are highly influential. <i>Eremitic</i> monks, or <a href="/wiki/Hermit" title="Hermit">hermits</a>, are those who live solitary lives. It is the yearning of many who enter the monastic life to eventually become solitary hermits. This most austere life is only granted to the most advanced monastics and only when their superiors feel they are ready for it.\n</p><p>Hermits are usually associated with a larger monastery but live in seclusion some distance from the main compound. Their local monastery will see to their physical needs, supplying them with simple foods while disturbing them as little as possible. In between are those in <i>semi-eremitic</i> communities, or <i>sketes</i>, where one or two monks share each of a group of nearby dwellings under their own rules and only gather together in the central chapel, or <i>katholikon</i>, for liturgical observances.\n</p><p>The spiritual insight gained from their ascetic struggles make monastics preferred for missionary activity. Bishops are almost always chosen from among monks, and those who are not generally receive the monastic tonsure before their consecrations.\n</p><p>Many (but not all) Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Seminary" title="Seminary">seminaries</a> are attached to monasteries, combining academic preparation for <a href="/wiki/Holy_Orders" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Orders">ordination</a> with participation in the community\'s life of prayer. Monks who have been ordained to the priesthood are called <i>hieromonk</i> (priest-monk); monks who have been ordained to the diaconate are called <i>hierodeacon</i> (deacon-monk). Not all monks live in monasteries, some hieromonks serve as priests in parish churches thus practicing "monasticism in the world".\n</p><p>Cultural practices differ slightly, but in general <i>Father</i> is the correct form of address for monks who have been tonsured, while Novices are addressed as <i>Brother</i>. Similarly, <i>Mother</i> is the correct form of address for nuns who have been tonsured, while Novices are addressed as <i>Sister</i>. Nuns live identical ascetic lives to their male counterparts and are therefore also called <i>monachoi</i> (monastics) or the feminine plural form in Greek, <i>monachai</i>, and their common living space is called a monastery.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Icons_and_symbols">Icons and symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Icons and symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_symbolism" title="Christian symbolism">Christian symbolism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rom,_Calixtus-Katakomben,_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Rom%2C_Calixtus-Katakomben%2C_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg/180px-Rom%2C_Calixtus-Katakomben%2C_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg" width="180" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Rom%2C_Calixtus-Katakomben%2C_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg/270px-Rom%2C_Calixtus-Katakomben%2C_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Rom%2C_Calixtus-Katakomben%2C_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg/360px-Rom%2C_Calixtus-Katakomben%2C_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1261" data-file-height="931" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rom,_Calixtus-Katakomben,_Steintafel_mit_Christussymbol_%22Chi_Rho%22.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "<a href="/wiki/Chi-Rho" class="mw-redirect" title="Chi-Rho">Chi-Rho</a>" symbol, <a href="/wiki/Catacomb_of_Callixtus" title="Catacomb of Callixtus">Catacombs of San Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg/180px-Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg" width="180" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg/270px-Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg/360px-Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1625" data-file-height="1686" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chrisme_Constantinople.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"IX" (Jesus Christ) monogram from a 4th-century <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> from <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG/180px-Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG" width="180" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG/270px-Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG/360px-Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_friday_in_Greek_Orthodox_church.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Easter eggs in front of altar, symbol of Resurrection.</div></div></div>\n<p>Everything in the Eastern Orthodox Church has a purpose and a meaning revealing God\'s revelation to man. At the front, or eastern end of the church, is a raised dais with an icon-covered screen or wall (<a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">iconostasis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Templon" title="Templon">templon</a>) separating the nave from the sanctuary. In the center of this wall is the entrance to the altar known as the "Royal Doors" through which only the clergy may pass.\n</p><p>There is a right and left side door on the front of the iconostasis, one depicting the archangel, Michael and the other Gabriel. The priest and altar boys enter and exit through these doors during appropriate parts of the Divine Liturgy. Immediately to the right of the main gate you will always find an icon of Jesus Christ, on the left, an icon of the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a> (Mother of God). Other icons depicted on the iconostasis are Saint John the Forerunner and the Saint after which the church is named.\n</p><p>In front of the iconostasis is the bishop\'s chair, a place of honor where a visiting bishop or metropolitan will often sit when visiting the church. An Orthodox priest, when standing at the altar during the Divine Liturgy, faces toward the altar (typically facing east) and properly leads his congregation while together they perform the mystical sacrifice and pray to God.\n</p><p>The sanctuary contains the Holy Altar, representing the place where Orthodox Christians believe that Christ was born of the virgin Mary, crucified under Pontius Pilate, laid in the tomb, descended into hell, rose from the dead on the third day, ascended into heaven, and will return again at his second coming. A free-standing cross, bearing the body of Christ, may stand behind the altar. On the altar are a cloth covering, a large book containing the gospel readings performed during services, an ark containing presanctified divine gifts (bread and wine) distributed by the deacon or priest to those who cannot come to the church to receive them, and several white beeswax candles.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Icons">Icons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Icons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">Icon</a></div>\n<p>The term \'icon\' comes from the Greek word <i>eikon</i>, which simply means image. The Eastern Orthodox believe that the first icons of Christ and <a href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">the Virgin Mary</a> were painted by <a href="/wiki/Luke_the_Evangelist" title="Luke the Evangelist">Luke the Evangelist</a>. Icons are filled with symbolism designed to convey information about the person or event depicted. For this reason, icons tend to be formulaic, following a prescribed methodology for how a particular person should be depicted, including hair style, body position, clothing, and background details.\n</p><p>Icon painting, in general, is not an opportunity for artistic expression, though each iconographer brings a vision to the piece. It is far more common for an icon to be copied from an older model, though with the recognition of a new saint in the church, a new icon must be created and approved. The personal and creative traditions of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Church art">Catholic religious art</a> were largely lacking in Orthodox icon painting before the 17th century, when Russian icons began to be strongly influenced by religious paintings and engravings from both Protestant and Catholic Europe. Greek icons also began to take on a strong western influence for a period and the difference between some Orthodox icons and western religious art began to vanish. More recently there has been a trend of returning to the more traditional and symbolic representations.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fedorovskaya3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Fedorovskaya3.jpg/170px-Fedorovskaya3.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Fedorovskaya3.jpg/255px-Fedorovskaya3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Fedorovskaya3.jpg/340px-Fedorovskaya3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fedorovskaya3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_St._Theodore" class="mw-redirect" title="Our Lady of St. Theodore">Our Lady of St. Theodore</a> (12th century), the protector of <a href="/wiki/Kostroma" title="Kostroma">Kostroma</a> and patron icon of the <a href="/wiki/Romanov" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanov">Romanov</a> family, following the Byzantine "Tender Mercy" type.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ConstantinoXI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ConstantinoXI.jpg/170px-ConstantinoXI.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ConstantinoXI.jpg/255px-ConstantinoXI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/ConstantinoXI.jpg 2x" data-file-width="298" data-file-height="393" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ConstantinoXI.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern icon depicting <a href="/wiki/Constantine_XI_Palaiologos" title="Constantine XI Palaiologos">Constantine XI Palaiologos</a>, last emperor of the Roman (Byzantine) empire</div></div></div>\n<p>Aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> borrow from the pre-Christian <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic art</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Henry_Chadwick_(theologian)" title="Henry Chadwick (theologian)">Henry Chadwick</a> wrote, "In this instinct there was a measure of truth. The representations of Christ as the Almighty Lord on his judgment throne owed something to pictures of Zeus. Portraits of the Mother of God were not wholly independent of a pagan past of venerated mother-goddesses. In the popular mind the saints had come to fill a role that had been played by heroes and deities."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large free-standing statues (three-dimensional depictions) are almost non-existent in the Eastern Orthodox Church. This is partly because <a href="/wiki/Cult_image" title="Cult image">cult images</a> of the Greek gods were a focus of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">ancient Greek religion</a> and its Roman equivalent, and much criticised by Early Christian writers, and partly because icons are meant to show the spiritual nature of man, not the sensual earthly body. <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">Reliefs</a>, however, were used in <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a>.\n</p><p>Icons are not considered by the Eastern Orthodox to be idols or objects of worship. The parameters of their usage were clearly spelled out by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">7th ecumenical council</a>. Justification for their usage utilises the following logic: before God took human form in Christ, no material depiction was possible and therefore blasphemous even to contemplate. Once God became incarnate, depiction was possible.\n</p><p>As Christ is believed to be God, it is justified to hold in one\'s mind the image of God-incarnate. Likewise, when one venerates an icon, it is not the wood or paint that are venerated but rather the individual shown, just as it is not the paper one loves when one might kiss the photograph of a loved one. As <a href="/wiki/Saint_Basil" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Basil">Saint Basil</a> famously proclaimed, honour or veneration of the icon always passes to its archetype. Following this reasoning, the veneration of the glorified human saint made in God\'s image, is always a veneration of the divine image, and hence God as foundational archetype.\n</p><p>Icons can be found adorning the walls of churches and often cover the inside structure completely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993271_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993271-136">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Most Orthodox homes have an area set aside for family prayer, usually an eastern facing wall, where are hung many icons. Icons have been part of Orthodox Christianity since the beginning of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Icons are often illuminated by a candle or oil lamp. (Beeswax for candles and olive oil for lamps are preferred because they are natural and burn cleanly.) Besides the practical purpose of making icons visible in an otherwise dark church, both candles and oil lamps symbolise the Light of the World, who is Christ.\n</p><p>Tales of miraculous icons are not uncommon, though it has always been considered that the message of such an event was for the immediate faithful involved and therefore does not usually attract crowds. Some miraculous icons whose reputations span long periods of time nevertheless become objects of pilgrimage along with the places where they are kept. As several Orthodox theologians and saints have explored in the past, the icon\'s miraculous nature is found not in the material, but in the glory of the saint who is depicted. The icon is a window, in the words of <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Florensky" title="Pavel Florensky">Paul Florensky</a>, that actually participates in the glory of what it represents.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iconostasis">Iconostasis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Iconostasis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">Iconostasis</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg/180px-Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg" width="180" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg/270px-Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg/360px-Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2178" data-file-height="3072" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_iconostasis_of_Annunciation_Cathedral_in_Moscow_01_by_shakko.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">Iconostasis</a> in the Cathedral of the Annunciation in the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>An <i>iconostasis</i>, also called the <i>templon</i>, is a wall of <a href="/wiki/Icons" class="mw-redirect" title="Icons">icons</a> and religious paintings, separating the <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary" title="Sanctuary">sanctuary</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">church</a>. <i>Iconostasis</i> also refers to a portable icon stand that can be placed anywhere within a church. The modern iconostasis evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine</a> <a href="/wiki/Templon" title="Templon">templon</a> in the 11th century. The evolution of the iconostasis probably owes a great deal to 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">Hesychast</a> <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a> and the wood-carving genius of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a>.\n</p><p>The first ceiling-high, five-leveled Russian iconostasis was designed by <a href="/wiki/Andrey_Rublyov" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrey Rublyov">Andrey Rublyov</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_the_Dormition" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of the Dormition">cathedral of the Dormition</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vladimir,_Russia" title="Vladimir, Russia">Vladimir</a> in 1408. The separation between sanctuary and nave accomplished by the iconostasis is not mandatory, though it is common practice. Depending on circumstance, the role of the iconostasis can be played by masonry, carved panels, screens, curtains, railings, a cord or rope, plain icons on stands, steps, or nothing at all.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross">Cross</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cross">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:92px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OrthodoxCross.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/OrthodoxCross.svg/90px-OrthodoxCross.svg.png" width="90" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/OrthodoxCross.svg/135px-OrthodoxCross.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/OrthodoxCross.svg/180px-OrthodoxCross.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="142" data-file-height="211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OrthodoxCross.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A three-bar cross.</div></div></div>\n<p>Depictions of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a> within the Eastern Orthodox Church are numerous and often highly ornamented, but its use does not extend to all Orthodox traditions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some carry special significance.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Tri-Bar Cross, popular in Russia, Ukraine, and Belarus, but common throughout the Eastern Orthodox world, seen to the left, has three bars. Its origins are in the early Byzantine Church of the 4th century AD.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The small top crossbar represents the sign that <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a> nailed above Christ\'s head. It often is inscribed with an acronym, "INRI", Latin, meaning "<a href="/wiki/INRI" class="mw-redirect" title="INRI">Jesus of Nazareth, King of the Jews</a>" or "INBI", Greek, "<a href="/wiki/INBI" class="mw-redirect" title="INBI">Jesus of Nazareth, King of the Jews</a>"; however, it is often replaced or amplified by the phrase "The King of Glory" in order to answer Pilate\'s statement with Christ\'s affirmation, "My Kingdom is not of this world".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>There is also a bottom slanting bar which has several explanations. Claims of evidence indicate that there was a small wooden platform for the crucified to stand on in order to support his weight; in Jesus\' case his feet were nailed side by side to this platform with one nail each in order to prolong the torture of the cross.\n</p><p>Implied evidence for this comes mainly from two sources, namely, the Bible (in order to cause the victim to die faster, his legs were broken so they could not support his weight and he would suffocate)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and iconography (all early depictions of the crucifixion show this arrangement, not the later with feet on top with single nail).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It has also been pointed out by some experts that the nailed hands of a body crucified in the manner often shown in modern secular art would not support the weight of the body and would tear through. A platform for the feet would relieve this problem.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>That the bottom bar is slanted has two explanations, to represent the very real agony which Christ experienced on the cross (a refutation of <a href="/wiki/Docetism" title="Docetism">Docetism</a>)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and to signify that the thief on Christ\'s right chose the right path while the thief on the left did not.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Other crosses associated with the Eastern Orthodox Church are the more traditional single-bar crosses, budded designs, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek cross">Greek cross</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Latin_cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin cross">Latin cross</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_cross" title="Jerusalem cross">Jerusalem cross</a> (cross patt\xc3\xa9e), <a href="/wiki/Celtic_cross" title="Celtic cross">Celtic crosses</a>, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> A common symbolism of the slanted foot stool is The foot-rest points up, toward Heaven, on Christ\xe2\x80\x99s right hand-side, and downward, to Hades, on Christ\xe2\x80\x99s left. "Between two thieves Thy Cross did prove to be a balance of\nrighteousness: wherefore one of them was dragged down to Hades by the\nweight of his blasphemy <i>[the balance points downward],</i> whereas the other was lightened of his transgressions unto the comprehension of theology <i>[the balance points upward].</i> O Christ God, glory to Thee."<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Another Orthodox cross which is worn in gold is an outer budded cross with an inner Three Bar Cross. The inscription Jesus Christ in Greek: IC (Iesous) on the left side bar and XC (Xhristos) on the right side bar, with a sun on the top of the cross. There is also typically an inscription on the back in <a href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Church Slavonic">Church Slavonic</a>: "\xd1\x81\xd0\xbf\xd0\xb0\xd1\x81\xd0\xb8 \xd0\xb8 \xd1\x81\xd0\xbe\xd1\x85\xd1\x80\xd0\xb0\xd0\xbd\xd0\xb8", "<i>Spasi i Sokhrani</i>", "<i>Save and Protect</i>". This cross is known as the Saint Olga Cross.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_church_architecture" title="Eastern Orthodox church architecture">Eastern Orthodox church architecture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg/240px-Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg/360px-Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg/480px-Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Orthodox-Church-interior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the traditional interior of an Orthodox church.</div></div></div>\n<p>The church building has many symbolic meanings; perhaps the oldest and most prominent is the concept that the Church is the Ark (as in <a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark" title="Noah&#39;s Ark">Noah</a>\'s) in which the world is saved from the flood of temptations; therefore, most Orthodox Churches are rectangular in design. Another popular configuration, especially for churches with large choirs is <a href="/wiki/Cruciform" title="Cruciform">cruciform</a> or cross-shaped or what is called the "Greek-cross."\n</p><p>Architectural patterns vary in shape and complexity, with chapels sometimes added around the main church, or triple altars; but in general, the symbolic layout of the church remains the same. Each church is created with specified qualifications based on what the apostles said in the Bible.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> These qualifications include how big the temple should be.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg/220px-%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg/330px-%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg/440px-%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4632" data-file-height="3464" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%CE%95%CF%83%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%9C%CE%B7%CE%BD%CE%AC_3604_hdr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of <a href="/wiki/Agios_Minas_Cathedral" title="Agios Minas Cathedral">Agios Minas Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heraklion" title="Heraklion">Heraklion</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Church building is divided into three main parts: the <a href="/wiki/Narthex" title="Narthex">narthex</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vestibule_(Architecture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vestibule (Architecture)">vestibule</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary" title="Sanctuary">sanctuary</a> (also called the <i>altar</i> or <i>holy place</i>). The narthex is where catechumens and non-Orthodox visitors were traditionally asked to stand during services. It is separated from the nave by "The Royal Gate". On each side of this gate are candle stands (menalia) representing the pillars of fire that went before the <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">Hebrew people</a> escaping from Egypt.\n</p><p>The nave is where most of the congregation stand during services. Traditionally, men stand on the right and women on the left. This is for a number of reasons: (1) Considering the family unit of past centuries the husband was dominant; thus, standing the same distance from the altar, equality is emphasised. (2) The idea of separating the sexes was inherited from the Jewish tradition of doing so within synagogues (3) Separation of sexes also followed the practice of choirs in which different levels of voice are placed in groups to facilitate harmony.\n</p><p>In general, men and women dress respectfully, typically wearing their "Sunday best" to enter the church. Often, women cover their heads as prescribed by Paul (1 Cor. 11:13). Children are considered full members of the Church and stand attentively and quietly during services. There is often a choir area at the side or in a loft in back. In addition to the Choir, a Chanter is always present at the front of the church to chant responses and hymns that are part of the Divine Liturgy offered by the Priest. There is usually a dome in the ceiling with an icon of Christ depicted as <a href="/wiki/Christ_Pantocrator" title="Christ Pantocrator">Ruler of the Universe</a> (Pantocrator).\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Archdiocesan_Cathedral_of_the_Holy_Trinity" title="Archdiocesan Cathedral of the Holy Trinity">Archdiocesan Cathedral of the Holy Trinity</a> on New York City\'s <a href="/wiki/Upper_East_Side" title="Upper East Side">Upper East Side</a> is the largest <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Christian">Orthodox Christian</a> church in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google1_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Apart from the icons, the Eastern Orthodox churches and monasteries are often decorated with <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">frescos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_customs">Local customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Local customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg/180px-Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg" width="180" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg/270px-Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg/360px-Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Broken_vases_on_Holy_Saturday_in_Corfu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shards of pottery vases on the street, after being thrown from the windows of nearby houses. A <a href="/wiki/Holy_Saturday" title="Holy Saturday">Holy Saturday</a> tradition in <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">Corfu</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church also has many associated traditions (sometimes referred to simply as customs), compatible with its life and function, but not necessarily tied so closely to the faith itself. These are not generally regarded as a part of Holy Tradition, though no strict dividing line is drawn. As long as compatibility is maintained, general practice often tends to the permissive rather than the restrictive, with the local priest or bishop resolving questions.\n</p><p>Many of these customs are local or cultural, and some are not even especially religious, but form a part of the church\'s relationship with the people in the time and place where it exists. Where outside customs affect church practices such as worship, a closer watch is kept for guarding the integrity of worship, but suitable local differences are welcomed and celebrated joyfully. The local church customs, especially liturgical ones, are referred to as differences in <a href="/wiki/Typica" title="Typica">typica</a> (Style).\n</p><p>Locality is also expressed in regional terms of churchly jurisdiction, which is often also drawn along national lines. Many Orthodox churches adopt a national title (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Autocephalous_Church_of_Albania" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Autocephalous Church of Albania">Albanian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_Orthodox_Church" title="Bulgarian Orthodox Church">Bulgarian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antiochian_Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiochian Orthodox Church">Antiochian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgian_Orthodox_Church" title="Georgian Orthodox Church">Georgian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegrin_Orthodox_Church" title="Montenegrin Orthodox Church">Montenegrin Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Romanian Orthodox Church">Romanian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Orthodox_Church" title="Serbian Orthodox Church">Serbian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Ukrainian Orthodox">Ukrainian Orthodox</a>, etc.) and this title can identify which language is used in services, which bishops preside, and which of the typica is followed by specific <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Congregation" class="extiw" title="wikt:Congregation">congregations</a>. In the Middle East, Orthodox Christians are usually referred to as <i>Rum</i> ("Roman") Orthodox, because of their historical connection with the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_(Byzantine)_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman (Byzantine) Empire">Eastern Roman (Byzantine) Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Binns2002p3_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Binns2002p3-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Differences in <i><a href="/wiki/Praxis_(Eastern_Orthodoxy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Praxis (Eastern Orthodoxy)">praxis</a></i> ("practice") tend to be slight, involving things such as the order in which a particular set of hymns are sung or what time a particular service is celebrated. But observances of the saints\' days of local saints are more often celebrated in special services within a locality, as are certain national holidays, like Greek Independence Day. In North America, observances of Thanksgiving Day are increasing.\n</p><p>Members of the Church are fully united in faith and the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Mysteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Mysteries">Sacred Mysteries</a> with all Orthodox congregations, regardless of nationality or location. In general, Orthodox Christians could travel the globe and feel familiar with the services even if they did not know the language being used.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, Christian Orthodox services and identity often combine both the Byzantine Greek and indigenous (Arabic and <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a>) traditions. Other Orthodox communities can identify with two Eastern Orthodox churches simultaneously, for example <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Greeks" title="Caucasus Greeks">Caucasus Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greeks</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Greeks in Russia">Russia</a> often identify with both the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a>, as a result of centuries of assimilation and intermarriage with ethnic Russians and other Christian Orthodox communities in mainly southern <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Holy_mysteries_.28sacraments.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_mysteries_(sacraments)">Holy mysteries (sacraments)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Holy mysteries (sacraments)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>According to Orthodox theology, the purpose of the Christian life is to attain <i><a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Orthodox_theology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theosis (Eastern Orthodox theology)">theosis</a></i>, the mystical union of mankind with God. This union is understood as both collective and individual. St. <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria" title="Athanasius of Alexandria">Athanasius of Alexandria</a> wrote concerning the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">Incarnation</a> that, "He (Jesus) was made man that we might be made god (\xce\xb8\xce\xb5\xce\xbf\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xce\xb9\xce\xb7\xce\xb8\xe1\xbf\xb6\xce\xbc\xce\xb5\xce\xbd)."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> See <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/2_Peter#1:4" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/2 Peter">2 Peter 1:4</a>, <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/John#10:34" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/John">John 10:34\xe2\x80\x9336</a>, <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#82:6" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">Psalm 82:6</a>. The entire life of the church is oriented towards making this possible and facilitating it.\n</p><p>In the Eastern Orthodox Church the terms "mystery" or "the mysteries" refer to the process of <i>theosis</i>. While it is understood that God theoretically can do anything instantly and invisibly, it is also understood that he generally chooses to use material substance as a medium in order to reach people. The limitations are those of mankind, not God. Matter is not considered to be evil by the Eastern Orthodox. Water, oil, bread, wine, etc., all are means by which God reaches out to allow people to draw closer to him. How this process works is a "mystery" and cannot be defined in human terms. These mysteries are surrounded by prayer and symbolism so that their true meaning will not be forgotten.\n</p><p>Those things which in the West are often termed <a href="/wiki/Sacraments" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacraments">sacraments</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sacramentals" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacramentals">sacramentals</a> are known among the Eastern Orthodox as the "sacred mysteries". While the Catholic Church numbers seven sacraments, and many Protestant groups list two (Baptism and the Eucharist) or even none, the Eastern Orthodox do not limit the number. However, for the sake of convenience, <a href="/wiki/Catechism" title="Catechism">catechisms</a> will often speak of the seven Great Mysteries. Among these are <a href="/wiki/Holy_Communion" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Communion">Holy Communion</a> (the most direct connection), <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrismation" title="Chrismation">Chrismation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">Confession</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unction" class="mw-redirect" title="Unction">Unction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matrimony" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrimony">Matrimony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">Ordination</a>. But the term also properly applies to other sacred actions such as monastic <a href="/wiki/Tonsure" title="Tonsure">tonsure</a> or the blessing of <a href="/wiki/Holy_water_in_Eastern_Christianity" title="Holy water in Eastern Christianity">holy water</a>, and involves fasting, almsgiving, or an act as simple as lighting a candle, burning incense, praying or asking God\'s blessing on food.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993274-277_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993274-277-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptism">Baptism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Baptism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg/220px-GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg/330px-GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg/440px-GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="666" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GreekOrthodoxBaptism1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Orthodox baptism</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a> is the Mystery which transforms the old and sinful person into a new and pure one; the old life, the sins, any mistakes made are gone and a clean slate is given. Through Baptism a person is united to the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_Christ" title="Body of Christ">Body of Christ</a> by becoming a member of the Orthodox Church. During the service, <a href="/wiki/Holy_water_in_Eastern_Christianity" title="Holy water in Eastern Christianity">water</a> is blessed. The catechumen is fully immersed in the water three times in the name of the Holy Trinity. This is considered to be a death of the "old man" by participation in the crucifixion and burial of Christ, and a rebirth into new life in Christ by participation in his resurrection.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993277-278_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993277-278-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Properly a new name is given, which becomes the person\'s name.\n</p><p>Children of Orthodox families are normally baptized shortly after birth. Converts to Orthodoxy are usually formally baptized into the Orthodox Church, though exceptions are sometimes made. Those who have left Orthodoxy and adopted a new religion, if they return to their Orthodox roots, are usually received back into the church through the Mystery of Chrismation.\n</p><p>Properly, the Mystery of Baptism is administered by bishops and priests; however, in emergencies any Orthodox Christian can baptize.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993278_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993278-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In such cases, should the person survive the emergency, it is likely that the person will be properly baptized by a priest at some later date. This is not considered to be a second baptism, nor is it imagined that the person is not already Orthodox, but rather it is a fulfillment of the proper form.\n</p><p>The service of Baptism used in Orthodox churches has remained largely unchanged for over 1500 years. This fact is witnessed to by St. <a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Jerusalem" title="Cyril of Jerusalem">Cyril of Jerusalem</a> (d. 386), who, in his <i>Discourse on the Sacrament of Baptism</i>, describes the service in much the same way as is currently in use.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chrismation">Chrismation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Chrismation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Chrismation" title="Chrismation">Chrismation</a> (sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmation</a><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>) is the Mystery by which a baptized person is granted the gift of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> through anointing with Holy <a href="/wiki/Chrism" title="Chrism">Chrism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993278-9_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993278-9-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> It is normally given immediately after baptism as part of the same service, but is also used to receive lapsed members of the Orthodox Church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarakas198756\xe2\x80\x937_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarakas198756\xe2\x80\x937-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> As baptism is a person\'s participation in the death and resurrection of Christ, so Chrismation is a person\'s participation in the coming of the Holy Spirit at <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ware1993p279_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993p279-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A baptized and chrismated Orthodox Christian is a full member of the Church and may receive the Eucharist regardless of age.<sup id="cite_ref-ware1993p279_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993p279-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The creation of chrism may be accomplished by any bishop at any time, but usually is done only once a year, often when a synod of bishops convenes for its annual meeting. (Some autocephalous churches get their chrism from others.) Anointing with it substitutes for the laying-on of hands described in the New Testament, even when an instrument such as a brush is used.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarakas198757_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarakas198757-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Holy_Communion_.28Eucharist.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_Communion_(Eucharist)">Holy Communion (Eucharist)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Holy Communion (Eucharist)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg/220px-Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg/330px-Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg/440px-Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Liturgy_St_James_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eucharistic elements prepared for the Divine Liturgy</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG/180px-%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG" width="180" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG/270px-%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG/360px-%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1052" data-file-height="1410" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%CE%9A%CE%BF%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CE%BD_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A0%CE%AC%CF%83%CF%87%CE%B1_-_%C2%AB%CE%A3%E1%BF%B6%CE%BC%CE%B1_%CE%A7%CF%81%CE%B9%CF%83%CF%84%CE%BF%E1%BF%A6_%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B5%CF%84%CE%B5%C2%BB.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An icon of Holy Communion: "Receive the Body of Christ; taste the Fountain of Immortality."</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> is at the center of Orthodox Christianity. In practice, it is the partaking of the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_Christ" title="Body of Christ">Body</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood_of_Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood of Jesus Christ">Blood of Jesus Christ</a> in the midst of the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a> with the rest of the church. The bread and wine are believed to become the genuine Body and Blood of the Christ <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> through the operation of the Holy Spirit. The Orthodox Church has never<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> described exactly how this occurs, or gone into the detail that the Catholic Church has in the West.\n</p><p>Communion is given only to baptized and chrismated Orthodox Christians who have prepared by fasting, prayer and confession. The priest will administer the gifts with a spoon, called a "cochlear", directly into the recipient\'s mouth from the chalice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWare1993287_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWare1993287-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> From baptism young infants and children are carried to the chalice to receive holy communion.<sup id="cite_ref-ware1993p279_150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993p279-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of the Orthodox understanding of mankind\'s fallen nature in general those who wish to commune prepare themselves in a way that reflects mankind in paradise. First, they prepare by having their confession heard and the prayer of repentance read over them by a priest. They will increase their prayer rule, adding the prescribed prayers in preparation for communing. Finally, they will fast completely from food and drink from the evening of the previous day (usually sunset on Saturday if communing on Sunday).\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Repentance_.28Confession.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Repentance_(Confession)">Repentance (Confession)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Repentance (Confession)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">Confession (religion)</a></div>\n<p>Orthodox Christians who have committed sins but repent of them, and who wish to reconcile themselves to God and renew the purity of their original <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptisms</a>, confess their sins to God before a spiritual guide who offers advice and direction to assist the individual in overcoming their sin. Parish priests commonly function as spiritual guides, but such guides can be any person, male or female, who has been given a blessing to hear confessions. Spiritual guides are chosen very carefully as this is a mandate that once chosen must be obeyed. Having confessed, the penitent then has his or her parish priest read the prayer of repentance over them.\n</p><p>Sin is not viewed by the Orthodox as a stain on the soul that needs to be wiped out, or a legal transgression that must be set right by a punitive sentence, but rather as a mistake made by the individual with the opportunity for spiritual growth and development. An act of <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">Penance</a> (<i>epitemia</i>), if the spiritual guide requires it, is never formulaic, but rather is directed toward the individual and their particular problem, as a means of establishing a deeper understanding of the mistake made, and how to effect its cure. Because full participatory membership is granted to infants, it is not unusual for even small children to confess; though the scope of their culpability is far less than an older child, still their opportunity for spiritual growth remains the same.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Marriage in the Eastern Orthodox Church">Marriage in the Eastern Orthodox Church</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_(1895,_Hermitage).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_%281895%2C_Hermitage%29.jpg/250px-Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_%281895%2C_Hermitage%29.jpg" width="250" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_%281895%2C_Hermitage%29.jpg/375px-Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_%281895%2C_Hermitage%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_%281895%2C_Hermitage%29.jpg/500px-Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_%281895%2C_Hermitage%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1433" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wedding_of_Nicholas_II_and_Alexandra_Feodorovna_by_Laurits_Tuxen_(1895,_Hermitage).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The wedding of Tsar <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Nicholas II of Russia</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>From the Orthodox perspective, marriage is one of the holy mysteries or sacraments. As well as in many other Christian traditions, for example in Catholicism, it serves to unite a woman and a man in eternal union and love before God, with the purpose of following Christ and His Gospel and raising up a faithful, holy family through their holy union.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The church understands marriage to be the union of one man and one woman, and certain Orthodox leaders have spoken out strongly in opposition to the civil institution of <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jesus said that "when they rise from the dead, they neither marry nor are given in marriage, but are like angels in heaven" (Mk 12:25). For the Orthodox Christian this passage should not be understood to imply that Christian marriage will not remain a reality in the Kingdom, but points to the fact that relations will not be "fleshy", but "spiritual".<sup id="cite_ref-meyendorff_70_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meyendorff_70-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Love between wife and husband, as an icon of relationship between Christ and Church, is eternal.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Church does recognize that there are rare occasions when it is better that couples do separate, but there is no official recognition of civil divorces. For the Orthodox, to say that marriage is indissoluble means that it should not be broken, the violation of such a union, perceived as holy, being an offense resulting from either adultery or the prolonged absence of one of the partners. Thus, permitting remarriage is an act of compassion of the Church towards sinful man.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Ecclesiastically divorced Orthodox (not civilly divorced only) are usually allowed to remarry in the Orthodox Church, though there is usually imposed on them a fairly severe penance by their bishop and the services for a second marriage in this case are more penitential than joyful. Widows are permitted to remarry without repercussion and their second marriage is considered just as valid as the first. One exception to this rule is the clergy and their wives. Should a married priest die, it is normal that his wife will retire to a monastery once their children are out of the house. Widowed priests are not allowed to remarry (no priest may be married after his ordination) and also frequently end up in monasteries.\n</p><p>The service of Marriage in the Orthodox Church has two distinct parts: the Betrothal and the Crowning. There is no exchange of vows. There is a set expectation of the obligations incumbent on a married couple, and whatever promises they may have privately to each other are their responsibility to keep.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_Orders">Holy Orders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Holy Orders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Orthodox_clergy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Orthodox_clergy.jpg/220px-Orthodox_clergy.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Orthodox_clergy.jpg/330px-Orthodox_clergy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Orthodox_clergy.jpg/440px-Orthodox_clergy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="992" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Orthodox_clergy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clergy at All Saints\' Antiochian Orthodox Church, Raleigh, United States (L to R): priest, two deacons, bishop.</div></div></div>\n<p>Since its founding, the Church spread to different places and its leaders in each region came to be known as <i>episkopoi</i> (overseers, plural of <i>episkopos</i>, overseer\xe2\x80\x94Gr. \xe1\xbc\x90\xcf\x80\xce\xaf\xcf\x83\xce\xba\xce\xbf\xcf\x80\xce\xbf\xcf\x82), which became "<a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a>" in English. The other <a href="/wiki/Holy_Orders" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Orders">ordained</a> roles are <i>presbyter</i> (Gr. <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xcf\x80\xcf\x81\xce\xb5\xcf\x83\xce\xb2\xcf\x8d\xcf\x84\xce\xb5\xcf\x81\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span>, elder), which became "prester" and then "<a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priest</a>" in English, and <i>diakonos</i> (Gr. <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xb4\xce\xb9\xce\xac\xce\xba\xce\xbf\xce\xbd\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</span>, servant), which became "<a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a>" in English (see also <a href="/wiki/Subdeacon" title="Subdeacon">subdeacon</a>). There are numerous administrative positions among the clergy that carry additional titles.\n</p><p>In the Greek tradition, bishops who occupy an ancient see are called metropolitans, while the lead bishop in Greece is the archbishop. (In the Russian tradition, however, the usage of the terms "metropolitan" and "archbishop" is reversed.) Priests can be archpriests, archimandrites or protopresbyters. Deacons can also be archdeacons or protodeacons. The position of deacon is often occupied for life. The deacon also acts as an assistant to a bishop.\n</p><p>With the exception of bishops, who remain <a href="/wiki/Celibacy" title="Celibacy">celibate</a>, the Orthodox Church has always allowed priests and deacons to be married, provided the marriage takes place before <a href="/wiki/Holy_Orders" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Orders">ordination</a>. In general it is considered preferable for parish priests to be married as they often act as counsel to married couples and thus can draw on their own experience. Unmarried priests usually are monks and live in monasteries, though there are occasions when, because of a lack of married priests, a monk-priest is temporarily assigned to a parish.\n</p><p>Widowed priests and deacons may not remarry and it is common for such members of the clergy to retire to a monastery (see <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy" title="Clerical celibacy">clerical celibacy</a>). This is also true of widowed wives of clergy, who do not remarry and become nuns when their children are grown. Only men are allowed to take holy orders, although deaconesses had both liturgical and pastoral functions within the church.<sup id="cite_ref-Karras_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karras-160">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> However, it has fallen out of practice (the last deaconess was ordained in the 19th century).\n</p><p>In 2017, Patriarch Theodoros II and the Holy Synod of the Patriarchate of Alexandria decided to reinstate the order of deaconesses in the Greek Orthodox Church.  In February, he appointed six nuns to be subdeacons within the church.  (Catherine Clark (2017).  "National Catholic Reporter."  March\xe2\x80\x93April 6, 2017.  p. 7 )\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unction">Unction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Unction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Anointing with oil, often called "unction", is one of the mysteries administered by the Orthodox Church and it is not reserved only for the dying or terminally ill, but for all in need of spiritual or bodily healing. In Greece, during the Ottoman occupation, it became the custom to administer this Mystery annually on <a href="/wiki/Holy_Wednesday" title="Holy Wednesday">Great Wednesday</a> to all believers; in recent decades, this custom has spread to many other locations. It is often distributed on major feast days, or any time the clergy feel it necessary for the spiritual welfare of its congregation.\n</p><p>According to Orthodox teaching unction is based on the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Is anyone among you sick? Let him call for the elders of the church, and let them pray over him, anointing him with oil in the name of the Lord. And the prayer of faith will save the sick, and the Lord will raise him up. And if he has committed sins, he will be forgiven.\xe2\x80\x94<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/James#5:14" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/James">James 5:14\xe2\x80\x9315</a></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Orthodox Church">History of the Orthodox Church</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Church">Early Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Early Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/200px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg" width="200" height="80" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/300px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/400px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1276" data-file-height="510" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early Christian "<a href="/wiki/Ichthys" title="Ichthys">Ichthys</a>" (<i>fish</i>) inscription from ancient <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Following Jesus Christ\'s <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a> to the apostles, Christianity spread rapidly throughout the Roman Empire. <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelve Apostles">Apostles</a> traveled extensively throughout the Empire, including Asia Minor, <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">establishing Churches in major communities</a>, with the first Churches appearing in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_in_Christianity" title="Jerusalem in Christianity">Jerusalem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>, then in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thessalonica" class="mw-redirect" title="Thessalonica">Thessalonica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Byzantium</a>, which centuries later would become prominent as the <a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">New Rome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ware1993_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Christianity in the Roman Empire met with considerable resistance, as its adherents would refuse to comply with the Roman state (even at the threat of death) in offering sacrifice to the pagan gods. Despite persecutions, the Church spread. The persecution dissipated upon the <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I and Christianity">conversion of Emperor Constantine I</a> in 324&#160;AD.<sup id="cite_ref-ware1993_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a> Christianity had spread to numerous regions. A number of influential schools of thought had arisen, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Catechetical_School_of_Alexandria" title="Catechetical School of Alexandria">Alexandrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/School_of_Antioch" title="School of Antioch">Antiochian</a> philosophical approaches. Other groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Arians" class="mw-redirect" title="Arians">Arians</a>, had also managed to gain influence. However, their positions caused theological conflicts within the Church, thus prompting the Emperor Constantine to call for a great ecumenical synod in order to define the Church\'s position against the growing, often widely diverging, philosophical and theological interpretations of Christianity. He made it possible for this council to meet not only by providing a location, but by offering to pay for the transportation of all the existing bishops of the Church. Most modern Christian Churches regard this synod, commonly called the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> or more generally the <a href="/wiki/First_Ecumenical_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="First Ecumenical Council">First Ecumenical Council</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ware1993_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ware1993-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> as of major importance.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecumenical_councils">Ecumenical councils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Ecumenical councils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_seven_Ecumenical_Councils" class="mw-redirect" title="First seven Ecumenical Councils">First seven Ecumenical Councils</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicaea_icon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/220px-Nicaea_icon.jpg" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/330px-Nicaea_icon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/440px-Nicaea_icon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1388" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nicaea_icon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Icon depicting <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">the Emperor Constantine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> of the First Council of Nicaea (325) holding the <a href="/wiki/Niceno-Constantinopolitan_Creed" class="mw-redirect" title="Niceno-Constantinopolitan Creed">Niceno\xe2\x80\x93Constantinopolitan Creed of 381</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Several doctrinal disputes from the 4th century onwards led to the calling of <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">Ecumenical councils</a>. In the Orthodox Church, an Ecumenical council is the supreme authority that can be invoked to resolve contested issues of the faith.
