As such, these councils have been held to resolve the most important theological matters that came to be disputed within the Church. Many lesser disagreements were resolved through local councils in the areas where they arose, before they grew significant enough to require an Ecumenical council.\n</p><p>There are seven councils authoritatively recognised as Ecumenical:\n</p>\n<ol><li>The <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Ecumenical Council</a> was convoked by the Roman Emperor Constantine at <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a> in 325 and presided over by the Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_I_of_Alexandria" title="Pope Alexander I of Alexandria">Alexander of Alexandria</a>, with over 300 bishops condemning the view of <a href="/wiki/Arius" title="Arius">Arius</a> that the Son is a created being inferior to the Father.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">Second Ecumenical Council</a> was held at Constantinople in 381, presided over by the Patriarchs of Alexandria and Antioch, with 150 bishops, defining the nature of the Holy Spirit against those asserting His inequality with the other persons of the Trinity.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Ephesus" class="mw-redirect" title="First Council of Ephesus">Third Ecumenical Council</a> is that of Ephesus in 431, presided over by the Patriarch of Alexandria, with 250 bishops, which affirmed that Mary is truly "Birthgiver" or "Mother" of God (<i><a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a></i>), contrary to the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Nestorius" title="Nestorius">Nestorius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Fourth Ecumenical Council</a> is that of Chalcedon in 451, Patriarch of Constantinople presiding, 500 bishops, affirmed that Jesus is truly God and truly man, without mixture of the two natures, contrary to <a href="/wiki/Monophysite" class="mw-redirect" title="Monophysite">Monophysite</a> teaching.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Second Council of Constantinople">Fifth Ecumenical Council</a> is the second of Constantinople in 553, interpreting the decrees of Chalcedon and further explaining the relationship of the two natures of Jesus; it also condemned the alleged teachings of <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> on the pre-existence of the soul, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Third Council of Constantinople">Sixth Ecumenical Council</a> is the third of Constantinople in 681; it declared that Christ has two wills of his two natures, human and divine, contrary to the teachings of the <a href="/wiki/Monothelites" class="mw-redirect" title="Monothelites">Monothelites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Seventh Ecumenical Council</a> was called under the Empress Regent <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Athens" title="Irene of Athens">Irene of Athens</a> in 787, known as the second of Nicaea. It supports the <a href="/wiki/Veneration" title="Veneration">veneration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icons</a> while forbidding their worship. It is often referred to as "The Triumph of Orthodoxy".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>There are also two other councils which are considered Ecumenical by some Orthodox. All Orthodox agree that the decisions of these further councils are valid; the disagreement is only whether they carry sufficient importance to be considered truly <i>Ecumenical:</i>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>8. The <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Constantinople_(Eastern_Orthodox)" title="Fourth Council of Constantinople (Eastern Orthodox)">Fourth Council of Constantinople</a> was called in 879. It restored St. <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photius</a> to his See in Constantinople and condemned any alteration of the Nicene-Constantinopolitan Creed of 381.</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>9. The <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Fifth Council of Constantinople">Fifth Council of Constantinople</a> was actually a series of councils held between 1341 and 1351. It affirmed the <a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">hesychastic</a> theology of St. <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Palamas" title="Gregory Palamas">Gregory Palamas</a> and condemned the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Barlaam_of_Calabria" class="mw-redirect" title="Barlaam of Calabria">Barlaam of Calabria</a>.</dd></dl>\n<p>In addition to these councils there have been a number of other significant councils meant to further define the Orthodox position. They are the Synods of Constantinople, in <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Constantinople_(1484)" title="Synod of Constantinople (1484)">1484</a>, 1583, <a href="/wiki/Oros_of_the_Holy_Great_Church_of_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Oros of the Holy Great Church of Christ">1755</a>, 1819, and 1872, the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jassy" title="Synod of Jassy">Synod of Jassy</a> (Ia\xc8\x99i) in 1642, and the Pan-Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem_(1672)" title="Synod of Jerusalem (1672)">Synod of Jerusalem in 1672</a>. Another council convened in <a href="/wiki/Pan-Orthodox_Council" title="Pan-Orthodox Council">June 2016</a> to discuss many modern phenomena including <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">Modernism</a>, other Christian confessions, Orthodoxy\'s relation with other religions and fasting disciplines.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Roman.2FByzantine_Empire"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman/Byzantine_Empire">Roman/Byzantine Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Roman/Byzantine Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aya_Sofya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Aya_Sofya.jpg/220px-Aya_Sofya.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Aya_Sofya.jpg/330px-Aya_Sofya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Aya_Sofya.jpg/440px-Aya_Sofya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="993" data-file-height="748" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aya_Sofya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, the largest church in the world and patriarchal basilica of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> for nearly a thousand years, later converted into a <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a>, now a museum</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople</a></div>\n<p>Eastern <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a> reached its golden age during the high point of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> and continued to flourish in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a>. Numerous <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> churches were established in Europe: Russia, Greece, Georgia and Ukraine, as well as Asia.\n</p><p>In the 530s the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Church of the Holy Wisdom</a> (Hagia Sophia) was built in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> under Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_schisms">Early schisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Early schisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Church in Egypt (<a href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchate of Alexandria">Patriarchate of Alexandria</a>) split into two groups following the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> (451), over a dispute about the relation between the divine and human natures of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>. Eventually this led to each group <a href="/wiki/Anathema#Religious_usage" title="Anathema">anathematizing</a> the other. Those that remained in communion with the other patriarchs (by accepting the Council of Chalcedon) are known today as the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Greek Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>, where the adjective "Greek" refers to their ties to the Greek-speaking culture of the Byzantine Empire. However, those who disagreed with the findings of the Council of Chalcedon were the majority in Egypt, and today they are known as the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>, having maintained a separate patriarchate. The Coptic Orthodox Church is currently the largest Christian church in Egypt and in the whole Middle East. There was also a similar, albeit smaller scale, split in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> (<a href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Antioch" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchate of Antioch">Patriarchate of Antioch</a>), which resulted in the separation of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Antioch" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Antioch">Byzantine Patriarchate of Antioch</a>.\n</p><p>Those who disagreed with the Council of Chalcedon are sometimes called "<a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodox</a>" to distinguish them from the "<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a>", who accepted the Council of Chalcedon. Oriental Orthodox are also sometimes referred to as "non-Chalcedonians", or "anti-Chalcedonians". The Oriental Orthodox Church denies that it is monophysite and prefers the term "<a href="/wiki/Miaphysite" class="mw-redirect" title="Miaphysite">miaphysite</a>", to denote the "united" nature of Jesus (two natures united into one) consistent with St. Cyril\'s theology: "The term union...signifies the concurrence in one reality of those things which are understood to be united" and "the Word who is ineffably united with it in a manner beyond all description" (<a href="/wiki/St._Cyril_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Cyril of Alexandria">St. Cyril of Alexandria</a> - <i>On the Unity of Christ</i>). Both the Eastern Orthodox and Oriental Orthodox churches formally believe themselves to be the continuation of the true church, although over the last several decades there has been considerable reconciliation and the prospect of reunification has been discussed.\n</p><p>As well, there are the "<a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian</a>" churches, which are Eastern Christian churches that keep the faith of only the first two ecumenical councils, i.e., the First Council of Nicaea and the First Council of Constantinople. "Nestorian" is an outsider\'s term for a tradition that predated the influence of <a href="/wiki/Nestorius" title="Nestorius">Nestorius</a>, the origin of which might lay in certain sections of the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Antioch" title="School of Antioch">School of Antioch</a> or via Nestorius\' teachers <a href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Mopsuestia" title="Theodore of Mopsuestia">Theodore of Mopsuestia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diodore_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Diodore of Tarsus">Diodore of Tarsus</a>. The "<a href="/wiki/Nestorian_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Nestorian Church">Nestorian Church</a>" is commonly referred to as "the Assyrian Church" or fully as the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_of_East_and_South_Slavs">Conversion of East and South Slavs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Conversion of East and South Slavs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Bulgaria" title="Christianization of Bulgaria">Christianization of Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Rus%27_Khaganate" title="Christianization of the Rus&#39; Khaganate">Christianization of the Rus\' Khaganate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Christianization of Kievan Rus&#39;">Christianization of Kievan Rus\'</a></div>\n<p>In the 9th and 10th centuries, Christianity made great inroads into pagan Europe, including <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus&#39;">Kievan Rus\'</a>. This work was made possible by the work of the Byzantine-era <a href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">saints Cyril and Methodius</a>. When king <a href="/wiki/Rastislav_of_Moravia" title="Rastislav of Moravia">Rastislav of Moravia</a> asked Byzantium for teachers who could minister to the Moravians in their own language, <a href="/wiki/Michael_III" title="Michael III">Byzantine emperor Michael III</a> chose these two brothers. Cyril and Methodius translated the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and many of the prayer books. As the translations prepared by them were copied by speakers of other dialects, the hybrid literary language <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a> was created. Originally sent to convert the Slavs of <a href="/wiki/Great_Moravia" title="Great Moravia">Great Moravia</a>, Cyril and Methodius were forced to compete with <a href="/wiki/Frankish_mythology" title="Frankish mythology">Frankish</a> missionaries from the Roman diocese. Their disciples were driven out of Great Moravia in AD 886.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg/280px-Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg" width="280" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg/420px-Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg/560px-Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="309" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radzivill_Olga_in_Konstantinopol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The baptism of <a href="/wiki/Olga_of_Kiev" title="Olga of Kiev">Princess Olga</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a></i>, a miniature from the <a href="/wiki/Radzivill_Chronicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Radzivill Chronicle">Radzivill Chronicle</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Some of the disciples, namely Saint <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Ohrid" title="Clement of Ohrid">Clement of Ohrid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Naum" title="Saint Naum">Saint Naum</a>, were of great importance to the Orthodox Faith in Bulgaria. In a short time, the disciples of Cyril and Methodius managed to prepare and instruct the future Bulgarian clergy into the biblical texts and in AD 893, proclaimed the first organized Church on the Balkan Peninsula. The success of the conversion of the Bulgarians facilitated the conversion of East <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic peoples</a>, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus&#39; people">Rus\'</a>, predecessors of <a href="/wiki/Belarusians" title="Belarusians">Belarusians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The work of the <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a> brothers Cyril and Methodius and their disciples had a major impact to <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> However, they accepted Christianity collectively by families and by tribes (in the process between the 7th and the 9th century). In commemoration of their baptisms, each Serbian family or tribe began to celebrate an exclusively Serbian custom called <a href="/wiki/Slava" title="Slava">Slava</a> (patron saint) in a special way to honor the Saint on whose day they received the sacrament of Holy <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a>. It is the most solemn day of the year for all Serbs of the Orthodox faith and has played a role of vital importance in the history of the Serbian people. Slava is actually the celebration of the spiritual birthday of the Serbian people which the Church blessed and proclaimed it a Church institution.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The missionaries to the East and <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">South Slavs</a> had great success in part because they used the people\'s native language rather than <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, the predominant language of the Byzantine Empire or <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> as the Roman priests did.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Today the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> is the largest of the Orthodox Churches followed by the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Romanian Orthodox Church">Romanian Orthodox Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Great_Schism_.281054.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Schism_(1054)">Great Schism (1054)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Great Schism (1054)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism">East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism</a></div>\n<p>In the 11th century what was recognised as the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East\xe2\x80\x93West Schism">Great Schism</a> took place between <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, which led to separation between the Church of the West, the Catholic Church, and the Eastern Byzantine Churches, now the Orthodox. There were doctrinal issues like the <a href="/wiki/Filioque_clause" class="mw-redirect" title="Filioque clause">filioque clause</a> and the authority of the Roman <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> involved in the split, but these were greatly exacerbated by political factors of both Church and state, and by cultural and linguistic differences between Latins and Greeks. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Papal_supremacy" title="Papal supremacy">Papal supremacy</a>, the Eastern half grew disillusioned with the Pope\'s centralization of power, as well as his blatant attempts of excluding the Eastern half in regard to Papacy approvals. It used to be that the Emperor would at least have say when a new Pope would be elected, but towards the high Middle Ages, the Christians in Rome were slowly consolidating power and removing Byzantine influence. However, even before this exclusionary tendency from the West, well before 1054, the Eastern and Western halves of the Church were in perpetual conflict, particularly during the periods of Eastern <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Photian_schism" title="Photian schism">Photian schism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg/220px-Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg" width="220" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg/330px-Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg/440px-Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1367" data-file-height="1315" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tintoretto.2tomaconstantinopla.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Latin Crusaders <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1204)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Constantinople (1204)">sacking the city of Constantinople</a>, the capital of the Eastern Orthodox controlled <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, in 1204.</div></div></div>\n<p>The final breach is often considered to have arisen after the capture and sacking of Constantinople by the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> in 1204; the final break with Rome occurred circa 1450. The sacking of <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Church of Holy Wisdom</a> and establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a> as a seeming attempt to supplant the Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> in 1204 is viewed with some rancour to the present day. In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> extended a formal apology for the sacking of Constantinople in 1204, which was importantly also strongly condemned by the Pope at the time (<a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Innocent III</a>, see reference at end of paragraph); the apology was formally accepted by <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Bartholomew_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarch Bartholomew of Constantinople">Patriarch Bartholomew of Constantinople</a>. Many things that were stolen during this time \xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">holy relics</a>, riches, and many other items\xe2\x80\x94were not returned and are still held in various European cities, particularly <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reunion was attempted twice, at the 1274 <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a> and the 1439 <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Florence" title="Council of Florence">Council of Florence</a>. The Council of Florence briefly reestablished communion between East and West, which lasted until after the fall of Constantinople in 1453. In each case, however, the councils were rejected by the Orthodox people as a whole, and the union of Florence also became very politically difficult after Constantinople came under Ottoman rule. Some local Eastern Churches have, however, renewed union with Rome in time since (see <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a>). Recent decades have seen a renewal of ecumenical spirit and dialogue between the Churches.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_Church_under_Ottoman_rule">Greek Church under Ottoman rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Greek Church under Ottoman rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Christianity in the Ottoman Empire">Christianity in the Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church_under_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="History of the Eastern Orthodox Church under the Ottoman Empire">History of the Eastern Orthodox Church under the Ottoman Empire</a></div>\n<p>In 1453, the Byzantine Empire fell to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. By this time <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> had been under <a href="/wiki/Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslims">Muslim</a> control for some seven centuries, but Orthodoxy was very strong in Russia which had recently acquired an <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> status; and thus <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> called itself the <a href="/wiki/Third_Rome#Russian_claims" title="Third Rome">Third Rome</a>, as the cultural heir of Constantinople.\n</p><p>Under Ottoman rule, the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Church of Constantinople">Greek Orthodox Church</a> acquired substantial power as an autonomous <i><a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet</a></i>. The ecumenical patriarch was the religious and administrative ruler of the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="R\xc3\xbbm">R\xc3\xbbm</a> (Ottoman administrative unit meaning "Roman"), which encompassed all the Orthodox subjects of the Empire.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_Orthodox_Church_in_the_Russian_Empire">Russian Orthodox Church in the Russian Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Russian Orthodox Church in the Russian Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Up until 1666, when Patriarch Nikon was deposed by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_tsar" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian tsar">tsar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> had been independent of the State.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> In 1721 the first Russian Emperor, <a href="/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter I of Russia">Peter I</a> abolished completely the patriarchate and so the Church effectively became a department of the government, ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Most_Holy_Synod" title="Most Holy Synod">Most Holy Synod</a> composed of senior bishops and lay bureaucrats appointed by the Emperor himself. From 1721 until the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolsheviks</a>\' <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a> of 1917, the Russian Orthodox Church was essentially transformed into a governmental agency, a tool used to various degrees by the tsars in the imperial campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a>. The Church was allowed by the State to levy <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxes</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a>. Therefore, the Church, along with the imperial regime, to which it belonged, came to be presented as an <a href="/wiki/Enemy_of_the_people" title="Enemy of the people">enemy of the people</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> and the other Russian revolutionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthodox_churches_under_Communist_rule">Orthodox churches under Communist rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Orthodox churches under Communist rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union">Persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A church being dynamited" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg/200px-Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg" width="200" height="153" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg/300px-Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg/400px-Christ_saviour_explosion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="610" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">1931 demolition of the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour" title="Cathedral of Christ the Saviour">Cathedral of Christ the Saviour</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Large church" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg/200px-Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg" width="200" height="267" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg/300px-Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg/400px-Russia-Moscow-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">The rebuilt Cathedral of Christ the Saviour, currently the tallest Orthodox church</div></div></div></div>\n<p>After the <a href="/wiki/October_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="October revolution">October revolution</a> of 1917, part of the clergy of the Russian Church escaped the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> persecutions by fleeing abroad, where they founded an <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church_Outside_Russia" title="Russian Orthodox Church Outside Russia">independent church in exile</a>, reunified with the Russian one in 2007.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Orthodox Church clergy in Russia were seen as sympathetic with the cause of the <a href="/wiki/White_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="White Army">White Army</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Civil War</a> after the Revolution, and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Collaborationism" title="Collaborationism">collaborated</a> with it; <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Tikhon_of_Moscow" title="Patriarch Tikhon of Moscow">Patriarch Tikhon</a>\'s declared position was vehemently anti-Bolshevik in 1918. This may have further strengthened the Bolshevik animus against the church.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Soviet government confiscated church property, ridiculed religion, harassed believers, and propagated atheism in schools.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Actions toward particular religions, however, were determined by State interests, and most organized religions were never outlawed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some actions against Orthodox priests and believers along with <a href="/wiki/Execution" class="mw-redirect" title="Execution">execution</a> included <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a>, being sent to <a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">prison camps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharashka" title="Sharashka">labour camps</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psikhushka" title="Psikhushka">mental hospitals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-blqarv_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blqarv-184">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In the first five years after the Bolshevik revolution, 28 bishops and 1,200 priests were executed.<sup id="cite_ref-time_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-186">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Nazi Germany\'s attack on the Soviet Union in 1941, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> revived the Russian Orthodox Church to intensify patriotic support for the war effort. By 1957 about 22,000 Russian Orthodox churches had become active. But in 1959 <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> initiated his own campaign against the Russian Orthodox Church and forced the closure of about 12,000 churches. It is estimated that 50,000 clergy had been executed between the revolution and the end of the Khrushchev era. Members of the church hierarchy were jailed or forced out, their places taken by docile clergy, many of whom had ties with the KGB. By 1985 fewer than 7,000 churches remained active.<sup id="cite_ref-time_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-186">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, there is definitely marked return to Christian Orthodoxy in Russia. According to the Pew Research Religion &amp; Public Life Project, between 1991 and 2008, the share of Russian adults identifying as Orthodox Christian rose from 31 percent to 72 percent, according to a new Pew Research Center analysis of three waves of data (1991, 1998 and 2008) from the International Social Survey Programme (ISSP) \xe2\x80\x93 a collaboration involving social scientists in about 50 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> was the only state to have declared itself <a href="/wiki/State_atheism" title="State atheism">officially fully atheist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In some other Communist states such as <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Romanian Orthodox Church">Romanian Orthodox Church</a> as an organization enjoyed relative freedom and even prospered, albeit under strict secret police control. That, however, did not rule out demolishing churches and monasteries as part of broader <a href="/wiki/Systematization_(Romania)" title="Systematization (Romania)">systematization</a> (urban planning), and state persecution of individual believers. As an example of the latter, Romania stands out as a country which ran a <a href="/wiki/Pite%C8%99ti_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Pite\xc8\x99ti prison">specialized institution</a> where many Orthodox (along with people of other faiths) were subjected to <a href="/wiki/Psychological_punishment" title="Psychological punishment">psychological punishment</a> or torture and <a href="/wiki/Mind_control" class="mw-redirect" title="Mind control">mind control</a> experimentation in order to force them give up their religious convictions. However, this was only supported by one faction within the regime, and lasted only three years. The Communist authorities closed down the prison in 1952, and punished many of those responsible for abuses (twenty of them were sentenced to death).<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_other_Christians">Relations with other Christians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Relations with other Christians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg/220px-Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg/330px-Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg/440px-Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="374" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Consecration_of_The_Right_Reverend_Weller.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The consecration of the Rt Rev. <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Heber_Weller" title="Reginald Heber Weller">Reginald Heber Weller</a> as an Anglican bishop at the <a href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Cathedral,_Fond_du_Lac" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Paul&#39;s Cathedral, Fond du Lac">Cathedral of St. Paul the Apostle</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Diocese_of_Fond_du_Lac" title="Episcopal Diocese of Fond du Lac">Episcopal Diocese of Fond du Lac</a>, with the Rt. Rev. Anthony Kozlowski of the <a href="/wiki/Polish_National_Catholic_Church" title="Polish National Catholic Church">Polish National Catholic Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Tikhon_of_Moscow" title="Patriarch Tikhon of Moscow">Saint Tikhon, then Bishop of the Aleutians and Alaska</a> (along with his chaplains Fr. <a href="/wiki/John_Kochurov" title="John Kochurov">John Kochurov</a> and Fr. Sebastian Dabovich) of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> present</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_(1).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_%281%29.JPG/220px-Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_%281%29.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_%281%29.JPG/330px-Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_%281%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_%281%29.JPG/440px-Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_%281%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pope_Franciscus_%26_Patriarch_Bartholomew_I_in_the_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre_in_Jerusalem_(1).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_I_of_Constantinople" title="Bartholomew I of Constantinople">Patriarch Bartholomew I</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Church of the Holy Sepulchre</a>, Jerusalem, 2014</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Muscovite_War_(1605%E2%80%9318)" title="Polish\xe2\x80\x93Muscovite War (1605\xe2\x80\x9318)">Polish\xe2\x80\x93Muscovite War (1605\xe2\x80\x9318)</a></div>\n<p>Eastern Orthodoxy represents the majority of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>. The Orthodox trace their bishops back to the apostles through <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">apostolic succession</a>, and continue the ancient Christian practices of veneration of saints, especially Mary the Mother of God as the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a>, prayers for the dead, and <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a>. Orthodoxy does not openly promote <a href="/wiki/Statuary" class="mw-redirect" title="Statuary">statuary</a>, although it is not expressly condemned, instead limiting itself primarily to two-dimensional iconography. Western theological concepts of <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substitutionary_atonement" title="Substitutionary atonement">substitutionary atonement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Particular_judgment" title="Particular judgment">particular judgment</a> are generally rejected by traditional Orthodox theologians.\n</p><p>The Orthodox believe themselves to be the <a href="/wiki/Four_Marks_of_the_Church" title="Four Marks of the Church">One Holy Catholic and Apostolic Church</a>, that is, the true Church established by Jesus Christ and placed into the care of the apostles. As almost all other Christian groups are in indirect schism with the Orthodox Church, mostly as a result of the <a href="/wiki/East-West_schism" class="mw-redirect" title="East-West schism">Great Schism</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> at the turn of the second Christian millennium (before the schisms of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>), these other groups are viewed as being Christian, but who, to varying degrees, lack full theological orthodoxy and <a href="/wiki/Orthopraxy" title="Orthopraxy">orthopraxy</a>. As such, all groups outside of the Orthodox Church are not seen as being members of the Church proper, but rather separated brethren who have failed to retain the fullness of the Christian faith and theology. These deviations from orthodoxy have traditionally been called <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>, but due to the term\'s perceived pejorative connotations, some prefer the more technical designation of the term <a href="/wiki/Heterodoxy" title="Heterodoxy">heterodoxy</a>.\n</p><p>In 1920, the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople</a>, published an encyclical "addressed \'To all the Churches of Christ, wherever they may be\', urging closer co-operation among separated Christians, and suggesting a \'League of Churches\', parallel to the newly founded <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Ware1993_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ware1993-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> This gesture was instrumental in the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a> (WCC);<sup id="cite_ref-Fuchs2008_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuchs2008-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> as such, almost all Eastern Orthodox Churches are members of the WCC and "Orthodox ecclesiastics and theologians serve on its committees".<sup id="cite_ref-Benz2008_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benz2008-193">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kallistos_Ware" title="Kallistos Ware">Kallistos Ware</a>, a British metropolitan bishop of the Orthodox Church, has stated that <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">ecumenism</a> "is important for Orthodoxy: it has helped to force the various Orthodox Churches out of their comparative isolation, making them meet one another and enter into a living contact with non-Orthodox Christians."<sup id="cite_ref-Diokleia)1993_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diokleia)1993-194">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hilarion_Alfeyev" class="mw-redirect" title="Hilarion Alfeyev">Hilarion Alfeyev</a>, Metropolitan of Volokolamsk and head of external relations for the Moscow Patriarchate of the Russian Orthodox Church, stated that Orthodox and <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelical Protestant</a> Christians share the same positions on "such issues as <a href="/wiki/Pro-life" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro-life">abortion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Family_values#Conservative_definitions" title="Family values">family</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_marriage" title="Christian views on marriage">marriage</a>" and desire "vigorous grassroots engagement" between the two <a href="/wiki/Communion_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Communion (Christian)">Christian communions</a> on such issues.<sup id="cite_ref-Communions_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Communions-195">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In that regard, the differences between the Catholic and Eastern Orthodox communions have not been improved in any relevant way. Dogmatic and <a href="/wiki/Liturgical" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical">liturgical</a> polarities have been significant, even and especially in recent times. A pertinent point of contention between the monarchically papal, administratively centralized Catholic Church and the decentralized confederation of Orthodox Churches is the theological significance of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a></i>, or "God-bearer").<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Even during a visit by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a> to <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> in October 2016, the leader of the Catholics was snubbed by most Orthodox Christians when he was holding mass in front of the practically empty <a href="/wiki/Mikheil_Meskhi_Stadium" title="Mikheil Meskhi Stadium">Mikheil Meskhi Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Oriental Orthodox Churches are not in <a href="/wiki/Communion_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Communion (Christian)">communion</a> with the Eastern Orthodox Church, despite their similar names. Slow dialogue towards restoring communion between the two churches began in the mid-20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-sor.cua.edu_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sor.cua.edu-198">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> and, notably, in the 19th century, when the Greek Patriarch in Egypt had to absent himself from the country for a long period of time; he left his church under the guidance of the Coptic <a href="/wiki/Pope_Cyril_IV_of_Alexandria" title="Pope Cyril IV of Alexandria">Pope Cyril IV of Alexandria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_Islam">Relations with Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Relations with Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg/220px-1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg/330px-1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg/440px-1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg 2x" data-file-width="908" data-file-height="650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1821_attrocities_Constantinople.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Constantinople_Massacre_of_1821" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantinople Massacre of 1821">Constantinople Massacre of April 1821</a>: a religious persecution of the Greek population of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> under the Ottomans. Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Gregory_V_of_Constantinople" title="Gregory V of Constantinople">Gregory V of Constantinople</a> was executed.</div></div></div>\n<p>Historically, the Orthodox Church and the non-Chalcedonians were among the first peoples to have contact with Islam, which conquered Roman/Byzantine Syria-Palestine and Egypt in the 7th century, and fought many battles against Islamic conquests. The Qur\'an itself records its concurrent observations regarding the Roman world in <a href="/wiki/Ar-R%C5%ABm" class="mw-redirect" title="Ar-R\xc5\xabm">Surah al-Rum</a>. The main contact with Islam however, came after the conquest of the Seljuk Turks of Roman/Byzantine Anatolia in the 13th century.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dhimma" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhimma">Christians who were under Islamic rule</a> were denied equality of rights and forced to pay the <a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">Jizya</a> poll tax.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Russia, Metropolitan <a href="/wiki/Hilarion_Alfeyev" class="mw-redirect" title="Hilarion Alfeyev">Alfeyev</a> stated belief in the possibility of peaceful coexistence between <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> as the two religions have never had religious wars in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-Alfeyev_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alfeyev-201">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Present">Present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(December 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The various autocephalous and autonomous synods of the Orthodox Church are distinct in terms of administration and local culture, but for the most part exist in <a href="/wiki/Full_communion" title="Full communion">full communion</a> with one another.\n</p><p>Presently, there are two communions that reject each other and, in addition, some schismatic churches not in any communion, all three groups identifying as Eastern Orthodox. The main traditional historical communion is divided into two groups - those who use the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Julian_calendar" title="Revised Julian calendar">Revised Julian calendar</a> for calculating fixed feasts and the Julian calendar for calculating movable feasts, and those who use the Julian calendar for all purposes. This second group may include congregations whose church allows them to choose, with the proviso that the choice remains in effect at least until the end of the church year. Also in communion are the Estonian and Finnish Orthodox churches who have a dispensation to use the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> for all purposes. Another group are referred to as <a href="/wiki/True_Orthodoxy" title="True Orthodoxy">True Orthodoxy</a> (also <a href="/wiki/Old_Calendarists" title="Old Calendarists">Old Calendarists</a>), they are those who, without authority from their parent churches, have continued to use the old <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>, claiming that the <a href="/wiki/Old_Calendarists" title="Old Calendarists">calendar reform</a> in the 1920s is in contravention of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_councils" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical councils">ecumenical councils</a>. Similarly, another group called <a href="/wiki/Old_Believers" title="Old Believers">Old Believers</a>, separated in 1666 from the official Russian Orthodox Church as a protest against church rite reforms introduced by <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Nikon_of_Moscow" title="Patriarch Nikon of Moscow">Patriarch Nikon of Moscow</a>. As Eastern Orthodox Christianity is both collegial and local in structure, there is no single organization called the "True Orthodox Church" nor is there official recognition among the "True Orthodox" as to who is properly included among them. While some unions have taken place even up to the present, the majority of True Orthodox are only secondarily concerned with reunion as opposed to preservation of Eastern Orthodox teaching. The calendar question reflects the dispute between those who wish to use a calendar which is reformed yet not Gregorian (effectively gaining the perceived benefits of the Gregorian calendar without disregarding the three anathemas issued against it in the sixteenth century), something which opponents consider unnecessary and damaging to continuity, and those who wish to maintain the traditional ecclesiastical calendar (which happens to be based on the Julian calendar), arguing that such a modern change goes against 1900 years of Church tradition and was in fact perpetrated without an ecumenical council, which would surely have rejected the idea.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%CE%A0%CE%B1%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A5%CF%80%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%BF%CF%8D_%CE%95%CE%BE%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8E%CE%BD_%CE%9D._%CE%9A%CE%BF%CF%84%CE%B6%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B7%CE%BD_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CE%BA%CE%B1%CE%B9_%CE%9C%CE%B5%CE%B3%CE%AC%CE%BB%CE%B7_%CE%A3%CF%8D%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%B4%CE%BF_%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%9F%CF%81%CE%B8%CF%8C%CE%B4%CE%BF%CE%BE%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%95%CE%BA%CE%BA%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1%CF%82,_%CE%9A%CF%81%CE%AE%CF%84%CE%B7,_16-27-6-2016_(27614224782).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/%CE%A0%CE%B1%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A5%CF%80%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%BF%CF%8D_%CE%95%CE%BE%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8E%CE%BD_%CE%9D._%CE%9A%CE%BF%CF%84%CE%B6%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B7%CE%BD_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CE%BA%CE%B1%CE%B9_%CE%9C%CE%B5%CE%B3%CE%AC%CE%BB%CE%B7_%CE%A3%CF%8D%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%B4%CE%BF_%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%9F%CF%81%CE%B8%CF%8C%CE%B4%CE%BF%CE%BE%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%95%CE%BA%CE%BA%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1%CF%82%2C_%CE%9A%CF%81%CE%AE%CF%84%CE%B7%2C_16-27-6-2016_%2827614224782%29.jpg/240px-thumbnail.jpg" width="240" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/%CE%A0%CE%B1%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A5%CF%80%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%BF%CF%8D_%CE%95%CE%BE%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8E%CE%BD_%CE%9D._%CE%9A%CE%BF%CF%84%CE%B6%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B7%CE%BD_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CE%BA%CE%B1%CE%B9_%CE%9C%CE%B5%CE%B3%CE%AC%CE%BB%CE%B7_%CE%A3%CF%8D%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%B4%CE%BF_%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%9F%CF%81%CE%B8%CF%8C%CE%B4%CE%BF%CE%BE%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%95%CE%BA%CE%BA%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1%CF%82%2C_%CE%9A%CF%81%CE%AE%CF%84%CE%B7%2C_16-27-6-2016_%2827614224782%29.jpg/360px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/%CE%A0%CE%B1%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A5%CF%80%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%BF%CF%8D_%CE%95%CE%BE%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8E%CE%BD_%CE%9D._%CE%9A%CE%BF%CF%84%CE%B6%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B7%CE%BD_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CE%BA%CE%B1%CE%B9_%CE%9C%CE%B5%CE%B3%CE%AC%CE%BB%CE%B7_%CE%A3%CF%8D%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%B4%CE%BF_%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%9F%CF%81%CE%B8%CF%8C%CE%B4%CE%BF%CE%BE%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%95%CE%BA%CE%BA%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1%CF%82%2C_%CE%9A%CF%81%CE%AE%CF%84%CE%B7%2C_16-27-6-2016_%2827614224782%29.jpg/480px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3573" data-file-height="2598" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%CE%A0%CE%B1%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%85_%CE%A5%CF%80%CE%BF%CF%85%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%BF%CF%8D_%CE%95%CE%BE%CF%89%CF%84%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8E%CE%BD_%CE%9D._%CE%9A%CE%BF%CF%84%CE%B6%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B7%CE%BD_%CE%91%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1_%CE%BA%CE%B1%CE%B9_%CE%9C%CE%B5%CE%B3%CE%AC%CE%BB%CE%B7_%CE%A3%CF%8D%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%B4%CE%BF_%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%9F%CF%81%CE%B8%CF%8C%CE%B4%CE%BF%CE%BE%CE%B7%CF%82_%CE%95%CE%BA%CE%BA%CE%BB%CE%B7%CF%83%CE%AF%CE%B1%CF%82,_%CE%9A%CF%81%CE%AE%CF%84%CE%B7,_16-27-6-2016_(27614224782).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pan-Orthodox_Council" title="Pan-Orthodox Council">Pan-Orthodox Council</a>, Kolymvari, Crete, Greece, June 2016</div></div></div>\n<p>The dispute has led to much acrimony, and sometimes even to violence. Following canonical precepts, some adherents of the old calendar have chosen to abstain from clerical intercommunion with those synods which have embraced the new calendar until the conflict is resolved. The monastic communities on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a> have provided the strongest opposition to the new calendar, and to <a href="/wiki/Modernism_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Modernism in the Catholic Church">modernism</a> in general, while still maintaining communion with their mother church.\n</p><p>Some latent discontent between different <a href="/wiki/National_church" title="National church">national churches</a> exists also in part due to different approach towards <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">ecumenism</a>. While the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople</a>, the Orthodox bishops in North America gathered into the <a href="/wiki/Standing_Conference_of_the_Canonical_Orthodox_Bishops_in_the_Americas" title="Standing Conference of the Canonical Orthodox Bishops in the Americas">Standing Conference of the Canonical Orthodox Bishops in the Americas</a> (SCOBA), Romanian bishops, and others are fairly open to dialogue with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, both conservative and moderate Old Calendarists, many of the monks of Mount Athos, several bishops of Russian, Serbian, and some of Greek and Bulgarian churches regard ecumenism as compromising essential doctrinal stands in order to accommodate <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations" title="List of Christian denominations">other Christians</a>, and object to the emphasis on dialogue leading to inter-communion; believing instead that Orthodox must speak the truth with love, in the hope of leading to the eventual conversion to Orthodoxy of <a href="/wiki/Heterodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterodox">heterodox</a> Christians.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="please rewrite this overlong sentence to avoid damaging reader&#39;s brain (April 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Eastern Orthodox Christians are <a href="/wiki/American_upper_class" title="American upper class">among the wealthiest</a> Christian denominations in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Leonhardt_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leonhardt-202">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> They also tend to be better educated than most other religious groups in America, having a high number of <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">graduate</a> (68%) and <a href="/wiki/Post-graduate" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-graduate">post-graduate</a> (28%) degrees per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Pew_Forum_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Pew_Forum-203">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church_Outside_Russia" title="Russian Orthodox Church Outside Russia">Russian Orthodox Church Outside Russia</a> (ROCOR) has recently united with the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Moscow Patriarchate</a> (MP); these two branches of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> had separated from each other in the 1920s due to the subjection of the latter to the hostile <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet regime</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Canonical_Communion_with_the_Moscow_Patriarchate" title="Act of Canonical Communion with the Moscow Patriarchate">Act of Canonical Communion</a>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_communion">Main communion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_Orthodox_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Main communion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Orthodox Church is a <a href="/wiki/Full_communion" title="Full communion">communion</a> of 14 <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> (that is, administratively completely independent) regional churches,<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> plus the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_in_America" title="Orthodox Church in America">Orthodox Church in America</a>, which is recognised as autocephalous only by the Russian, Bulgarian, Georgian, Polish, the Czech-Slovak churches. Each has defined geographical boundaries of its jurisdiction and is ruled by its council of bishops or <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synod</a> presided by a senior bishop \xe2\x80\x93 its primate (or first hierarch). The primate may carry the honorary title of Patriarch, Metropolitan (in the Slavic tradition) or Archbishop (in the Greek tradition).\n</p><p>Each regional church consists of constituent eparchies (or, dioceses) ruled by a bishop. Some churches have given an eparchy or group of eparchies varying degrees of autonomy (self-government). Such autonomous churches maintain varying levels of dependence on their mother church, usually defined in a Tomos or other document of autonomy.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG/220px-Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG/330px-Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG/440px-Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_2010.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Pe%C4%87_(monastery)" title="Patriarchate of Pe\xc4\x87 (monastery)">Patriarchate of Pe\xc4\x87</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>, the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Orthodox_Church" title="Serbian Orthodox Church">Serbian Orthodox Church</a> from the 14th century when its status was upgraded into a patriarchate</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AlexGreekCathedralFront.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AlexGreekCathedralFront.jpg/200px-AlexGreekCathedralFront.jpg" width="200" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AlexGreekCathedralFront.jpg/300px-AlexGreekCathedralFront.jpg 1.5x, Evangelismos">Cathedral of Evangelismos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a
